The Second Possibility: Parts Differ in Essence but Share One Existence
Presents and refutes the second possibility with objections and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that parts differ in essence from the composite yet share one existence leads to impossible consequences.

Core Idea
Two essences cannot share one existence without absurdity.

The second possibility attempts to reconcile the composite and its parts by claiming they differ in essence but unite in existence. This seems plausible, but it faces two strong objections: it either forces one thing to inhere in multiple substrates, or it makes the whole exist without its parts. Both are impossible. The author also examines a reply to the second objection and its ultimate failure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the possibility
Read the opening statement: the parts differ in essence from the composite but exist with one existence. Notice that composition is said to be prior to existence.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection: if one existence subsists in each part separately, then one thing would inhere in multiple substrates. Why is this impossible? Reflect on the commentary.
3
Identify the second objection
If instead the existence subsists in the aggregate, then the whole exists without the parts having their own existence. This also leads to absurdity.
4
Examine the answer and its rebuttal
Someone might answer that the parts need only a non-independent existence. But the rebuttal shows this fails: if parts have no existence at all, then the composite is made from non-existent things, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الثاني : أن تكون تلك الصور لأمور مختلفة الماهية، إلا أنها موجودة في الخارج بوجود واحد وهذا هو القول بأن الأجزاء المحمولة تغاير المركب ماهية لا وجوداً ويرد عليه أن ذلك الوجود الواحد إن قام بكل واحدة من تلك الماهيات لزم حلول شيء واحد بعينه في محال متعددة، وإن قام بمجموعها من حيث هو لزم وجود الكل بدون وجود أجزائه، وكلاهما محال

قوله : ( إلا أنها موجودة في الخارج بوجود واحد فالتركيب متقدم على الوجود كما سيجيء.

قوله : (لزم حلول شيء واحد إلخ) أي ما هو في قوة الحلول؛ إذ يتصور الحلول في الوجود الذي هو أمر اعتباري، فإن اتصاف شيئين بأمر واحد متشخص محال؛ لأنه حكم بوحدة الاثنين، سواء كان ذلك أمراً موجوداً أو لا، قال الإمام في المباحث المشرقية : اعلم أن الهو هو من وجه، والمغايرة من وجه والمغايرة من وجه آخر فإذا قلنا للإنسان إنه حيوان فالمغايرة هاهنا حاصلة؛ لأن ماهية الحيوان غير ماهية الإنسان والاتحاد حاصل في الوجود، فإنه ليس الحيوان موجوداً والإنسان موجوداً آخر، بل الحيوان الموجود هو الإنسان بعينه. وهذا فيه نوع غموض ؛ فإنه كيف يمكن أن يكون للماهيتين وجود واحد وتقريره أن الحيوان لا يوجد إلا وأن يكون مقيداً بقيد إما الناطقية أو اللاناطقية، فإنه يستحيل أن يكون في الوجود حيوان لا ناطق ولا لا ناطق، ويجب أن يكون تقييده بأحد هذين القيدين سابقاً على وجوده؛ لأنه يستحيل يوجد مطلقاً ثم يتقيد، بل يتقيد أولاً ثم يوجد ، وإذا كان كذلك فالوجود إنما يعرض لذلك أن

قوله : لزم وجود الكل بدون وجود أجزائه أجيب عنه : بمنع لزوم الوجود الاستقلالي في الأجزاء؛ لجواز الاكتفاء فيه بوجود غير استقلالي لها وأنت خبير بأن لا وجود لها على هذا لا استقلالاً ولا تبعاً، إذ لم يقم بها وجود أصلاً ، ولو جعل وجود الكل وجوداً لها تبعاً من الفرض غير أن يقوم بها وجود أصلاً لجاز تركب الموجود من المعدوم، وذا باطل قطعاً.
The second possibility: that those forms are for things of different essences, except that they exist externally with one existence. This is the statement that the predicated parts differ from the composite in essence not in existence. It is objected that if that one existence subsists in each of those essences, then the inherence of one identical thing in multiple substrates would be necessary, and if it subsists in their aggregate as such, then the existence of the whole without the existence of its parts would be necessary, and both are impossible. His statement: 'Except that they exist externally with one existence, so composition is prior to existence as will come.' His statement: 'The inherence of one thing etc.' – meaning what is in the power of inherence, because inherence is conceived in existence which is a mental consideration, for the characterization of two things by one individuated thing is impossible because it is a judgment of the unity of two things, whether that thing is existent or not. Al-Imam said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: Know that identity (huwa huwa) is from one aspect, and difference is from another aspect. So when we say of man that he is an animal, difference occurs here because the essence of animal is other than the essence of man, and union occurs in existence, for the animal that exists is not a different existent from man, but the animal that exists is exactly man. This contains a kind of ambiguity: how can two essences have one existence? Its explanation is that animal does not exist except as qualified by a qualification, either rationality or non-rationality, for it is impossible for an animal to exist neither rational nor non-rational; its qualification by one of these two qualifications must be prior to its existence, because it is impossible for it to exist absolutely and then become qualified; rather, it becomes qualified first and then exists. And if so, then existence only accrues to that... His statement: 'The existence of the whole without the existence of its parts necessarily follows' – it is answered by denying the necessity of independent existence in the parts, because it is permissible to suffice with non-independent existence for them. But you are aware that they have no existence in this view, neither independently nor dependently, since no existence subsists in them at all. If the existence of the whole were made an existence for them dependently, assuming that no existence subsists in them at all, then the composition of the existent from the non-existent would be allowed, and that is absolutely false.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second possibility: that the parts differ in essence from the composite but share a single existence. Two objections are raised. First, if that one existence inheres in each essence individually, it would entail one thing inhering in multiple substrates, which is impossible. Second, if it inheres in the aggregate, then the whole exists without its parts, also impossible. The commentary then explains that the first objection is about inherence (hulul), which is impossible because characterizing two things by one individuated aspect would require a judgment of their unity. The author quotes Imam al-Razi to illustrate: when we say 'man is an animal', there is difference in essence but union in existence, yet this is ambiguous because animal always comes qualified (rational or non-rational). The qualification must precede existence, showing the difficulty. For the second objection, the answer is that there is no need for the parts to have independent existence; but the reply counters that if parts have no existence at all, making the whole's existence their existence would be like composing an existent from non-existent, which is absurd. Thus the second possibility is rejected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two different essences can share one existence without any issue.
Correction: This leads to either a single thing inhering in multiple substrates or the whole existing without parts, both impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection to the second possibility that parts differ in essence but share one existence?
A) It would require the parts to be identical to the composite in essence.
B) It would entail the inherence of one thing in multiple substrates.
C) It would make existence prior to composition.
D) It would deny the existence of the whole.

Key Takeaway
The second possibility fails because it leads to either one thing inhering in multiple substrates or the whole existing without its parts, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am a believer', do you feel that your essence (who you are) is identical to your belief, or do they differ? How does the logical impossibility of two essences sharing one existence challenge you to examine the consistency of your own self-understanding?
?
Al-Razi notes that animal cannot exist without being qualified (rational or non-rational). Think about your own existence: are there qualities that define you so essentially that without them you would not exist as 'you'? What does that reveal about your dependence on Allah for every aspect of your being?
?
The author dismisses the idea that parts can have non-independent existence, insisting they must have some reality. Consider your actions: are they really a part of you, or do they have no existence apart from you? If your actions have no independent existence, how can you be held accountable for them? Let this question press on your sense of moral responsibility.

Are Parts Just Mental Abstractions? The Third Possibility Refuted
States and refutes the view that parts are merely abstracted from the external thing and have no external reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third possibility about parts and how the author refutes it, establishing that parts are real and identical to the whole in existence.

Core Idea
Parts are not mere mental abstractions; they are identical to the whole in reality.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection to the reality of parts in composite things. The third possibility claims that parts are only conceptually abstracted and have no external reality. The author rejects this by showing that parts are identical to the whole, multiplicity is only mental, and the intellect can truly grasp the external thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement of the third possibility
Carefully read the opening lines describing the third possibility. Notice the three claims it makes: parts are external to the constitution, the intellect only grasps abstractions, and the essence is stripped of these parts.
This sets up the opponent's position.
2
Understand the refutation's structure
The author refutes each claim one by one. Track the 'we do not concede' (لا نسلم) statements that begin each counterpoint.
There are four main refutation points.
3
Grasp the key arguments
Focus on why the author says parts are identical to the whole in existence, and why the intellect can attain the external thing via a mirror concept.
This resolves the apparent conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الثالث: أن تلك الأجزاء خارجة عن قوام الأمر الخارجي منتزعة منه فيكون تسميته بالجزء مجرد اصطلاح، وأن يكون العقل لا ينال ما هو معروض الموجود الخارجي حقيقة، بل الأمور المنتزعة، وأن تكون تلك الذات البسيطة الشخصية مسلوباً عنها هذه الأشياء من حيث هي كما في العوارض، والكل مدفوع لأنا لا نسلم أن الأجزاء معدومة فإنها عين الكل متحدة معه في الجعل والوجود، إنما التعدد في الذهن، ولا نسلم خروجه عن قوام الأمر الخارجي مطلقاً بل في الخارج، ونحن نعترف به، إنما القوام بها في الذهن فتكون أجزاء حقيقة؛ لتقومه بها في الذهن، ولا نسلم أن العقل لا ينال الأمر الخارجي فإن نيل الأمر الخارجي ليس إلا أن يحصل في الذهن ما هو مرآة لمشاهدة نفسه وهو متحقق. وإن أردت معنى آخر فلا نسلم لزومه ولا نسلم جواز سلبها عنها، نعم إذا لوحظ كل واحدة منها مفصلة جاز سلبها عنها، لكن هذه المرتبة متأخرة عن الماهية من حيث هي كما مر .
The third possibility: that those parts are external to the constitution of the external thing, abstracted from it, so calling it a part is merely terminological; and that the intellect does not attain what is the substrate of the external existent in reality, but rather the abstracted matters; and that that simple personal essence is stripped of these things as such, as in the case of accidents. And all of this is rejected, for we do not concede that the parts are non-existent, for they are identical to the whole, united with it in making and existence; multiplicity is only in the mind. And we do not concede that it is outside the constitution of the external thing absolutely; rather, it is in the external [sense that] we acknowledge it; rather, the constitution is through them in the mind, so they are real parts because the whole is constituted by them in the mind. And we do not concede that the intellect does not attain the external thing, for attaining the external thing is nothing but the obtaining in the mind of that which is a mirror for observing itself, and that is realized. And if you intend another meaning, we do not concede its necessity, nor do we concede the permissibility of stripping them from it. Yes, when each one of them is observed in detail, it is permissible to strip them from it, but this level is posterior to the essence as such, as has passed.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage states and refutes the third possibility: that the parts are not part of the external constitution of the composite but are merely abstracted by the mind, so calling them 'parts' is just a convention. Additionally, the intellect cannot truly grasp the external existent itself but only abstracted notions; and the simple individual essence is stripped of these parts as accidents are stripped from their subjects. The author rejects all these claims. First, he asserts that parts are not nonexistent; they are identical to the whole in making and existence, with multiplicity only in the mind. Second, he denies that parts are outside the constitution of the external thing absolutely; rather, they are in the external thing in the sense that the mind constitutes the composite through them. Third, he denies that the intellect cannot attain the external thing: attaining the external thing means having in the mind a mirror that reflects it, which is realized. Finally, he allows that when parts are considered in detail they can be conceptually stripped from the essence, but that is a posterior mental operation, not reflecting the essence as such.

Key Terms
قوام
منتزعة
اصطلاح
معروض
مرآة
نيل
سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Parts are merely mental constructs with no external reality whatsoever.
Correction: The author argues that parts are identical to the whole in existence; they are real, and multiplicity is only in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the third possibility, why are the parts not non-existent?
A) Because they exist separately from the whole.
B) Because they are identical to the whole in making and existence.
C) Because they are merely conceptual abstractions.
D) Because the intellect creates them.

Key Takeaway
Parts are real and identical to the whole; mental abstraction does not negate their reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own character traits (e.g., kindness, anger), do you treat them as mere mental labels or as real parts of who you are? How does this lesson challenge you to accept their reality?
?
The intellect can attain external reality through a 'mirror' in the mind. Have you ever doubted that your knowledge truly corresponds to reality? What would change in your actions if you fully believed your intellect can know the truth?
?
The author says parts are identical to the whole in existence. How does this affect your understanding of your own unity as a person? Are you willing to accept that you are not a collection of separate pieces but a unified whole?

The Ambiguity of Unity in Predication — A Critique
A critique of the commentator's refutation on the type of unity required for predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator's refutation fails unless it proves that predication requires union in existence or identity.

Core Idea
Unity is ambiguous; predication may only need union in essence, not existence.

This passage contains a sharp critique of an earlier commentator's argument. The critic points out that the opponent in the original debate only required a weak form of unity (in essence), not the strong unity (in existence or identity) that the commentator assumed. Since unity is an ambiguous term, the commentator's refutation misses its mark.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Focus on the contrast between two types of unity.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الاتحاد بوجه من الوجوه' (union in some respect).
2
Identify the two types of unity
The passage distinguishes between union in some respect (e.g., essence) and union in existence or identity. Note which one the opponent uses and which the commentator assumes.
The critic argues the opponent only requires the weaker union.
3
Understand ambiguous (mushakkak) terms
The critic invokes the concept of 'al-waḥda al-mushakkaka' — unity is an ambiguous term applied to various kinds. This weakens the commentator's assumption that only one kind matters.
Ambiguous terms apply to multiple meanings; unity can be in relation, essence, etc.
4
See why the refutation fails
The commentator's refutation is only effective if he can prove that predication requires union in existence or identity. Since he hasn't, the original argument stands.
The critique hinges on proving a specific requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبهذا يبطل إلخ ) لا يخفى أن المستفاد من التمسك المذكور أن هذا القائل يعتبر في الحمل الاتحاد بوجه من الوجوه، حيث اكتفى فيه بحصول الذات الواحدة منها لا الاتحاد في الوجود أو في الهوية وسيجيء أن الوحدة مشكك يقال على الوحدة بأي وجه كانت حتى على الوحدة في النسبة، فيصح أن تلك الأمور المتغايرة ماهية ووجوداً متحدة باعتبار الذات، فما ذكره الشارح قدس سره لا يبطل هذا التمسك ولا يفيد رد المذهب المذكور إلا بعد إثبات أن الحمل يقتضي الاتحاد في الوجود أو الهوية .
His statement: (And by this is invalidated, etc.) It is not hidden that what is understood from the mentioned argument is that this speaker considers in predication a union (ittiḥād) in some respect, since he is content with the attainment of a single essence from them, not union in existence or identity (huwiyya). And it will come that unity (al-waḥda) is ambiguous (mushakkak), applied to unity in any manner, even unity in relation. So it is valid that those things which differ in essence and existence are united with respect to the essence. Thus, what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned does not invalidate this argument nor does it benefit the refutation of that doctrine except after proving that predication requires union in existence or identity.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis (naqd) of the commentator's previous refutation. The critic argues that the opponent in the original argument did not require union in existence or identity for predication; he only required a single essence resulting from the parts. The critic points out that unity is an ambiguous term (mushakkak) and can apply to various kinds, including unity in relation. Therefore, the commentator's refutation is ineffective unless he proves that predication specifically requires union in existence or identity. The passage highlights a subtlety about the nature of unity required for predication.

Key Terms
الاتحاد
الوحدة المشككة
الحمل
الهوية
المذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that any union suffices for predication, but the critic argues that the proponent may consider a different kind of unity.
Correction: The correct understanding is that the opponent only requires union in essence, not in existence or identity. The commentator's refutation only works if he proves that predication demands the stronger union.

Checkpoint
What does the critic say is required for the commentator's refutation to be valid?
A) Proving that the parts are not distinct in essence.
B) Proving that predication requires union in existence or identity.
C) Showing that unity is ambiguous.
D) Demonstrating that the whole is not real.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's refutation fails because he assumes a stronger type of unity than what the opponent actually required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own understanding of Tawheed, have you ever assumed a particular type of unity (e.g., existential) without considering that unity might be ambiguous? How does this lesson challenge you to be more precise in your beliefs about divine attributes?
?
The commentator's refutation failed because he imposed a stronger requirement than necessary. Are there areas in your practice of Islam where you add extra conditions beyond what is required by the Quran and Sunnah? What would it take to let go of those added burdens?
?
This passage shows that even scholars can misunderstand an opponent's position. When you disagree with someone, do you first take care to understand exactly what they mean by key terms, or do you rush to refute based on your own assumptions? How can you change this starting today?

Identity and the Restriction to Essential Attributes
Restriction of predication to essential attributes via identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why predication in kalam is restricted to essential attributes and why non-existential and accidental matters are excluded based on the concept of identity (huwiyya).

Core Idea
Identity anchors predication to essence, excluding accidents and non-existents.

The commentator begins by clarifying the scope of predication: it is only valid for essential attributes (dhātiyyāt) of existing essences. This restriction hinges on the concept of identity (huwiyya), which refers to the particular essence. Accidents like 'white' are part of the identity of the accident itself, not the subject, while non-existents have no identity at all. This lesson unpacks that foundational restriction.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic text from 'قوله : ( دون الأمور العدمية ودون ،العرضيات' up to 'فالقصر في إنما قوله : ( في الذاتيات) أي ذاتيات الماهيات الموجودة'. Also read the translation provided.
Notice the double exclusion: non-existential matters and accidental matters like 'white human'.
2
Understand identity as particular essence
The passage states that identity (huwiyya) is 'the particular essence' (الماهية الجزئية). For example, 'white' belongs to the identity of whiteness, not the human. This explains why predication is restricted.
Recall that 'identity' here is a technical term, not the everyday sense.
3
Grasp the relative restriction
The commentary notes that the restriction is relative (إضافي) and suggests that saying 'only valid in the predication of existents' would be clearer. This highlights that predication fundamentally deals with existents.
4
Reflect on the objection and answer about identity definition
A hypothetical objector points out that the commentator elsewhere defined identity as 'the essence of which the thing is predicated'. If that definition were used here, predication of non-existents might be allowed. The answer clarifies that that definition was metaphorical and context-specific.
This shows the importance of distinguishing technical terms from metaphorical usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( دون الأمور العدمية ودون ،العرضيات مثل الإنسان أبيض، لأن الهوية كما مر عبارة عن الماهية الجزئية، ولا شك أن الأبيض معتبر في هوية البياض دون الإنسان، فالقصر في إنما قوله : ( في الذاتيات) أي ذاتيات الماهيات الموجودة . قوله : (دون الأمور العدمية فيه تنبيه على أن الحصر في قوله : إنما يصح في الذاتيات إضافي، ولو قال : إنما يصح في حمل الوجودات لكان أظهر فإن قلت: الشارح فسر الهوية في جواب شبه القادحين في البديهيات بذات صدق عليه الشيء، فليكن المراد به في التعريف هذا المعنى، فلا يرد حمل العدميات . قلت : إطلاق هوية الشيء على ذات صدق عليه ذلك الشيء إطلاق مجازي والشارح إنما فسر الهوية بذلك في قول المصنف : وحمل الموجود على السواد للغاية مفهوماً والاتحاد هوية لضرورة أن مفهوم الوجود معقول ثان لا هوية له، فلا يلتفت إلى ذلك التفسير في مقام التعريف . قوله : (إذ ليس لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية لأن مبدأ الاشتقاق داخل في مفهوم المشتق، وهو هاهنا أمر عدمي والمركب من الموجود والمعدوم لا وجود له أصلاً، فلا يلتفت إلى ما يقال : مفهوم الأعمى من له العمى فيعبر عنه بمن حصل له هوية . فإن قلت : الأعمى وإن لم يكن له هوية خارجية محققة لم يضر في صدق التعريف على حمله على زيد؛ إذ يكفي الهوية المقدرة، كما أشار إليه المصنف بقوله : أو الموهومة، فمعنى حمله على زيد أنهما متحدان هوية، على تقدير أن يتحقق للمحمول هوية. قلت: لما امتنع أن يكون لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية جاز أن يدعى أنها على تقدير تحققها غير متحدة بهوية زيد مع صحة حمله عليه، لجواز استلزام المحال محالاً آخر. قوله : ( أن المتغايرين مفهوماً متحدان ذاتاً قال الشارح في حواشي التجريد : يرد عليه أن الأمور المتغايرة في المفهوم إذا تغايرت في الوجود أيضاً، لم يصح حمل بعضهما على بعض بالمواطأة، كما يشهد به البداهة وفيه بحث ظاهر؛ فإن الأمور المتغايرة في الوجود لا يمكن اتحادها بحسب الذات، أي ما صدقت هي عليه، اللهم إلا أن يحمل كلامه على أن الحمل لو في الذات لجاز حمل بعض الأمور المتغابرة في الوجود على بعض، إذا تحقق الاتحاد الذاتي ولو بحسب الفرض أيضاً، كما أن الإنسان لما كان عبارة عن الحيوان  كان عبارة عن  الاتحاد
His statement: (except for non-existential matters and accidental matters like 'a white human', because identity (huwiyya), as has passed, is an expression for the particular essence, and there is no doubt that 'white' is considered in the identity of whiteness, not of the human. So the restriction in 'only' is additive. His statement: (in the essential attributes) i.e., the essential attributes of existing essences. His statement: (except for non-existential matters) contains an indication that the restriction in his statement 'only valid in essential attributes' is relative, and if he had said 'only valid in the predication of existents' it would be clearer. For if you say: The commentator interpreted identity in the answer to the doubt of the objectors against self-evident matters as the essence of which the thing is predicated, so let the intended meaning in the definition be this sense, and then the predication of non-existents would not be problematic. I say: Applying the term 'identity of a thing' to the essence of which that thing is predicated is a metaphorical application, and the commentator only interpreted identity in that way in the statement of the author: 'And the predication of the existent on blackness is for the utmost unity of concept and union in identity due to the necessity that the concept of existence is a secondary intelligible with no identity', so no attention is paid to that interpretation in the context of definition. His statement: (For the concept of 'blind' has no external identity because the basis of derivation is included in the concept of the derived term, and it here is a non-existential matter, and a composite of existent and non-existent has no existence at all, so no attention is paid to what is said: the concept of 'blind' is 'one who has blindness', thus it is expressed by 'one who has obtained an identity'. For if you say: The 'blind' even if it has no actual external identity, it does not harm the applicability of the definition to its predication on Zayd, since the presumed identity suffices, as the author indicated by his statement: 'or the imagined', so the meaning of predicating it on Zayd is that they are united in identity, on the presumption that the predicate has an identity. I say: Since it is impossible for the concept of 'blind' to have an external identity, it is permissible to claim that on the presumption of its realization, it is not united with the identity of Zayd despite the validity of its predication on him, because the entailment of an impossibility may entail another impossibility. His statement: (That two things differing in concept are united in essence) The commentator said in the glosses of al-Tajrid: It is objected to it that things differing in concept, if they also differ in existence, it is not valid to predicate one of the other by agreement (muwāṭa'a), as the self-evident [intuition] bears witness to. And in [this objection] there is an apparent discussion; for things differing in existence cannot be united in respect of essence, i.e., what they are true of, unless his statement is interpreted to mean that predication, even if it is in essence, would allow predicating some things differing in existence of others if the essential union is realized, even hypothetically, as when 'human' was an expression for 'animal' was an expression for union.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an expansive commentary on the conditions for predication, focusing on the role of identity (huwiyya). It begins by analyzing the commentator's restriction of predication to essential attributes (dhātiyyāt), excluding non-existential and accidental matters. The commentator notes that identity is the particular essence, so in 'a white human', 'white' is part of the identity of whiteness, not of the human. The restriction is relative. A question arises: the commentator elsewhere interpreted identity as the essence of which a thing is predicated; why not use that here? The answer is that that interpretation is metaphorical and only used in a specific context. The example of 'blind' (aʿmā) is then examined: 'blind' has no external identity because it derives from a non-existential basis (blindness). Even if we assume a presumed identity, it does not guarantee union with Zayd's identity, as impossibility may follow from impossibility. Finally, a quote from the commentator's glosses on al-Tajrid objects that things differing in existence cannot be essentially united, but the passage suggests that essential union might be considered hypothetically. The text is cut off at the end.

Key Terms
الهوية
الماهية الجزئية
الذاتيات
الأمور العدمية
العرضيات
الحمل
الإضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That predication of accidents is valid because they are properties of the subject.
Correction: Accidents like 'white' are not part of the subject's identity; they belong to the identity of the accident itself, so predication of accidents does not meet the identity condition.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage exclude accidental matters like 'a white human' from valid predication?
A) Because 'white' is not an essential attribute of the human.
B) Because 'white' is part of the identity of whiteness, not of the human.
C) Because 'white' is a non-existent.
D) Because 'human' is not an accident.

Key Takeaway
Predication in kalam is valid only for essential attributes because identity requires the predicate to share the subject's particular essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that predication based on identity requires the predicate to share the subject's essence. Reflect on the attributes you use to describe yourself—your wealth, status, appearance. Do these accidental attributes truly belong to your essence as a servant of Allah? What would it mean for you to define yourself by your essential attributes (iman, taqwa) instead? Commit to one action today that reinforces your essential identity.
?
The commentator notes that the restriction to essential attributes is relative, not absolute. In your spiritual life, are there any areas where you have taken a relative ruling and made it absolute, causing rigidity? How can you humbly re-examine your understanding without abandoning core truths? Identify one such area and resolve to seek deeper knowledge.
?
Non-existential matters like blindness are excluded from predication because they have no identity. Is there a 'non-existential' matter in your life—a false belief, a harmful habit—that you are treating as real and defining yourself by? How can you strip it of its false identity and focus on what truly exists in your relationship with Allah?

Predicating Non-Existents: The Example of 'Blind'
Argument against predication of non-existents using 'blind'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of 'blind' (a non-existent derived term) cannot be validly predicated of a subject through identity, even with a presumed identity.

Core Idea
Non-existents lack external identity, so predication fails despite presumed union.

A key test case for the restriction to existents is the predication of terms derived from non-existents, like 'blind'. The concept of 'blind' includes a non-existential basis (blindness), so it has no external identity. Even if we imagine an identity for it, impossibility prevents its union with the subject. This lesson explores the detailed objection and answer on this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'blind'
Read the Arabic from 'قوله : (إذ ليس لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية' up to 'لجواز استلزام المحال محالاً آخر'. Also read the translation.
Focus on the objection ('فإن قلت') and the answer ('قلت').
2
Understand why 'blind' has no external identity
The passage argues that the concept of 'blind' is derived from 'blindness', which is a non-existential matter. The basis of derivation (مبدأ الاشتقاق) is included in the concept, and since it is non-existent, the composite of existent (the subject) and non-existent cannot have existence. Hence no external identity.
This is based on the principle that a composite of existent and non-existent has no existence.
3
Analyze the objection about presumed identity
The objector says that even if 'blind' has no actual identity, we can presume one (as the author mentioned 'or imagined'). In predication, we can consider them united in identity on that presumption. Why not accept predication then?
The objector is trying to salvage predication of non-existents by appealing to a hypothetical identity.
4
Grasp the answer: impossibility chains
The answer states: since it is impossible for 'blind' to have an external identity, even on the presumption of its realization, it is not necessarily united with Zayd's identity. The entailment of an impossibility (the presumption) may entail another impossibility (non-union), so predication does not hold.
This is a logical argument: from impossibility anything follows, including the failure of union.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( دون الأمور العدمية ودون ،العرضيات مثل الإنسان أبيض، لأن الهوية كما مر عبارة عن الماهية الجزئية، ولا شك أن الأبيض معتبر في هوية البياض دون الإنسان، فالقصر في إنما قوله : ( في الذاتيات) أي ذاتيات الماهيات الموجودة . قوله : (دون الأمور العدمية فيه تنبيه على أن الحصر في قوله : إنما يصح في الذاتيات إضافي، ولو قال : إنما يصح في حمل الوجودات لكان أظهر فإن قلت: الشارح فسر الهوية في جواب شبه القادحين في البديهيات بذات صدق عليه الشيء، فليكن المراد به في التعريف هذا المعنى، فلا يرد حمل العدميات . قلت : إطلاق هوية الشيء على ذات صدق عليه ذلك الشيء إطلاق مجازي والشارح إنما فسر الهوية بذلك في قول المصنف : وحمل الموجود على السواد للغاية مفهوماً والاتحاد هوية لضرورة أن مفهوم الوجود معقول ثان لا هوية له، فلا يلتفت إلى ذلك التفسير في مقام التعريف . قوله : (إذ ليس لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية لأن مبدأ الاشتقاق داخل في مفهوم المشتق، وهو هاهنا أمر عدمي والمركب من الموجود والمعدوم لا وجود له أصلاً، فلا يلتفت إلى ما يقال : مفهوم الأعمى من له العمى فيعبر عنه بمن حصل له هوية . فإن قلت : الأعمى وإن لم يكن له هوية خارجية محققة لم يضر في صدق التعريف على حمله على زيد؛ إذ يكفي الهوية المقدرة، كما أشار إليه المصنف بقوله : أو الموهومة، فمعنى حمله على زيد أنهما متحدان هوية، على تقدير أن يتحقق للمحمول هوية. قلت: لما امتنع أن يكون لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية جاز أن يدعى أنها على تقدير تحققها غير متحدة بهوية زيد مع صحة حمله عليه، لجواز استلزام المحال محالاً آخر. قوله : ( أن المتغايرين مفهوماً متحدان ذاتاً قال الشارح في حواشي التجريد : يرد عليه أن الأمور المتغايرة في المفهوم إذا تغايرت في الوجود أيضاً، لم يصح حمل بعضهما على بعض بالمواطأة، كما يشهد به البداهة وفيه بحث ظاهر؛ فإن الأمور المتغايرة في الوجود لا يمكن اتحادها بحسب الذات، أي ما صدقت هي عليه، اللهم إلا أن يحمل كلامه على أن الحمل لو في الذات لجاز حمل بعض الأمور المتغابرة في الوجود على بعض، إذا تحقق الاتحاد الذاتي ولو بحسب الفرض أيضاً، كما أن الإنسان لما كان عبارة عن الحيوان  كان عبارة عن  الاتحاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a presumed identity is sufficient for predication of non-existents.

Why does the answer reject the objection about presumed identity for 'blind'?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use words like 'blind' or 'ignorant' to describe yourself or others, do you ever pause to consider whether the attribute truly has an independent existence outside your mind? How might this lesson expose the gap between your words and reality, and what does that demand of you in terms of honesty?
?

?

Does your speech about the unseen—like the attributes of Allah—require a different kind of grounding? How does this lesson push you to refine your theological vocabulary?

Union of Concepts Differing in Existence
Discussion on essential union despite existential difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether things differing in concept can be essentially united if they also differ in existence, and the hypothetical consideration of essential union.

Core Idea
Things differing in existence cannot be essentially united unless hypothetically.

The final part of the passage shifts to a broader principle: two things that differ in concept and also existentially distinct cannot be predicated of each other by agreement (muwāṭa'a). The commentator engages with an objection from the glosses on al-Ṭajrīd, suggesting that essential union might be possible hypothetically even for existentially distinct things. This lesson unpacks that subtle argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Read the Arabic from 'قوله : ( أن المتغايرين مفهوماً متحدان ذاتاً' to the end 'كان عبارة عن الاتحاد'. Also read the translation. Note that the last line is cut off.
The text is incomplete; we work with what is given.
2
Identify the claim and objection
The claim: two things differing in concept are united in essence. The objection from the commentator's glosses: if they also differ in existence, predication by agreement is invalid (as self-evident). The passage then says there is an 'apparent discussion' in that objection.
The commentator seems to disagree with the objection, or at least finds it debatable.
3
Understand the hypothetical essential union
The passage suggests that the objection might be interpreted as allowing predication if essential union is realized, even hypothetically (like 'human' being an expression for 'animal' and union).
This is a nuance: essential union could be hypothetical, not necessarily real.

قوله : ( دون الأمور العدمية ودون ،العرضيات مثل الإنسان أبيض، لأن الهوية كما مر عبارة عن الماهية الجزئية، ولا شك أن الأبيض معتبر في هوية البياض دون الإنسان، فالقصر في إنما قوله : ( في الذاتيات) أي ذاتيات الماهيات الموجودة . قوله : (دون الأمور العدمية فيه تنبيه على أن الحصر في قوله : إنما يصح في الذاتيات إضافي، ولو قال : إنما يصح في حمل الوجودات لكان أظهر فإن قلت: الشارح فسر الهوية في جواب شبه القادحين في البديهيات بذات صدق عليه الشيء، فليكن المراد به في التعريف هذا المعنى، فلا يرد حمل العدميات . قلت : إطلاق هوية الشيء على ذات صدق عليه ذلك الشيء إطلاق مجازي والشارح إنما فسر الهوية بذلك في قول المصنف : وحمل الموجود على السواد للغاية مفهوماً والاتحاد هوية لضرورة أن مفهوم الوجود معقول ثان لا هوية له، فلا يلتفت إلى ذلك التفسير في مقام التعريف . قوله : (إذ ليس لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية لأن مبدأ الاشتقاق داخل في مفهوم المشتق، وهو هاهنا أمر عدمي والمركب من الموجود والمعدوم لا وجود له أصلاً، فلا يلتفت إلى ما يقال : مفهوم الأعمى من له العمى فيعبر عنه بمن حصل له هوية . فإن قلت : الأعمى وإن لم يكن له هوية خارجية محققة لم يضر في صدق التعريف على حمله على زيد؛ إذ يكفي الهوية المقدرة، كما أشار إليه المصنف بقوله : أو الموهومة، فمعنى حمله على زيد أنهما متحدان هوية، على تقدير أن يتحقق للمحمول هوية. قلت: لما امتنع أن يكون لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية جاز أن يدعى أنها على تقدير تحققها غير متحدة بهوية زيد مع صحة حمله عليه، لجواز استلزام المحال محالاً آخر. قوله : ( أن المتغايرين مفهوماً متحدان ذاتاً قال الشارح في حواشي التجريد : يرد عليه أن الأمور المتغايرة في المفهوم إذا تغايرت في الوجود أيضاً، لم يصح حمل بعضهما على بعض بالمواطأة، كما يشهد به البداهة وفيه بحث ظاهر؛ فإن الأمور المتغايرة في الوجود لا يمكن اتحادها بحسب الذات، أي ما صدقت هي عليه، اللهم إلا أن يحمل كلامه على أن الحمل لو في الذات لجاز حمل بعض الأمور المتغابرة في الوجود على بعض، إذا تحقق الاتحاد الذاتي ولو بحسب الفرض أيضاً، كما أن الإنسان لما كان عبارة عن الحيوان  كان عبارة عن  الاتحاد
His statement: (except for non-existential matters and accidental matters like 'a white human', because identity (huwiyya), as has passed, is an expression for the particular essence, and there is no doubt that 'white' is considered in the identity of whiteness, not of the human. So the restriction in 'only' is additive. His statement: (in the essential attributes) i.e., the essential attributes of existing essences. His statement: (except for non-existential matters) contains an indication that the restriction in his statement 'only valid in essential attributes' is relative, and if he had said 'only valid in the predication of existents' it would be clearer. For if you say: The commentator interpreted identity in the answer to the doubt of the objectors against self-evident matters as the essence of which the thing is predicated, so let the intended meaning in the definition be this sense, and then the predication of non-existents would not be problematic. I say: Applying the term 'identity of a thing' to the essence of which that thing is predicated is a metaphorical application, and the commentator only interpreted identity in that way in the statement of the author: 'And the predication of the existent on blackness is for the utmost unity of concept and union in identity due to the necessity that the concept of existence is a secondary intelligible with no identity', so no attention is paid to that interpretation in the context of definition. His statement: (For the concept of 'blind' has no external identity because the basis of derivation is included in the concept of the derived term, and it here is a non-existential matter, and a composite of existent and non-existent has no existence at all, so no attention is paid to what is said: the concept of 'blind' is 'one who has blindness', thus it is expressed by 'one who has obtained an identity'. For if you say: The 'blind' even if it has no actual external identity, it does not harm the applicability of the definition to its predication on Zayd, since the presumed identity suffices, as the author indicated by his statement: 'or the imagined', so the meaning of predicating it on Zayd is that they are united in identity, on the presumption that the predicate has an identity. I say: Since it is impossible for the concept of 'blind' to have an external identity, it is permissible to claim that on the presumption of its realization, it is not united with the identity of Zayd despite the validity of its predication on him, because the entailment of an impossibility may entail another impossibility. His statement: (That two things differing in concept are united in essence) The commentator said in the glosses of al-Tajrid: It is objected to it that things differing in concept, if they also differ in existence, it is not valid to predicate one of the other by agreement (muwāṭa'a), as the self-evident [intuition] bears witness to. And in [this objection] there is an apparent discussion; for things differing in existence cannot be united in respect of essence, i.e., what they are true of, unless his statement is interpreted to mean that predication, even if it is in essence, would allow predicating some things differing in existence of others if the essential union is realized, even hypothetically, as when 'human' was an expression for 'animal' was an expression for union.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an expansive commentary on the conditions for predication, focusing on the role of identity (huwiyya). It begins by analyzing the commentator's restriction of predication to essential attributes (dhātiyyāt), excluding non-existential and accidental matters. The commentator notes that identity is the particular essence, so in 'a white human', 'white' is part of the identity of whiteness, not of the human. The restriction is relative. A question arises: the commentator elsewhere interpreted identity as the essence of which a thing is predicated; why not use that here? The answer is that that interpretation is metaphorical and only used in a specific context. The example of 'blind' (aʿmā) is then examined: 'blind' has no external identity because it derives from a non-existential basis (blindness). Even if we assume a presumed identity, it does not guarantee union with Zayd's identity, as impossibility may follow from impossibility. Finally, a quote from the commentator's glosses on al-Tajrid objects that things differing in existence cannot be essentially united, but the passage suggests that essential union might be considered hypothetically. The text is cut off at the end.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That things differing in existence can never be predicated of each other.
Correction: The passage suggests that if essential union is realized (even hypothetically), predication might be allowed despite existential difference.

Checkpoint
What is the 'apparent discussion' (بحث ظاهر) in the objection from al-Tajrid?
A) The objection is obviously true.
B) The objection is false because predication of existentially different things is always valid.
C) The objection is not conclusive because essential union can be considered hypothetically.
D) The objection concerns only accidents, not essences.

Key Takeaway
Essential union may allow predication even for things differing in existence, at least hypothetically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the attributes of Allah—are they conceptually distinct yet existentially one with His essence—do you ever slip into treating them as separate 'things' in your heart? How does this lesson's notion of hypothetical essential union challenge you to deepen your tawhid beyond mere mental categories? What concrete step will you take today to remind yourself of this reality during dhikr?
?
Think of a relationship in your life where someone's words (concept) seem to contradict their actions (existence). How does the idea that essential union could be hypothetically realized affect your judgment of them? Will you still rush to accuse, or will you consider the possibility of a deeper unity? Commit to one specific person you will extend this understanding to this week.
?
You know that your own soul has both a drive towards good and a pull towards evil—conceptually opposite. This lesson suggests that even things differing in existence can be united in essence hypothetically. Do you believe that your deepest essence (your fitrah) can reclaim that hypothetical unity with righteousness? What is one lie of the self (nafs) you need to stop believing that keeps you from pursuing that union starting today?

Definition of Predication and the Existence Objection
Definition of predication with objection and hypothetical defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of predication as two different concepts united in essence and the objection that if concepts differ in existence, predication is impossible unless essential union is assumed hypothetically.

Core Idea
Predication requires union in essence even if concepts differ, but existence differences challenge that.

This lesson begins with the definition of predication as 'two concepts different in concept but united in essence'. Sanusi introduces a criticism: if the two concepts also differ in existence, they cannot be predicated of each other uniformly. He then considers a saving interpretation involving hypothetical essential union, using the example of man as rational animal.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the definition of predication and the commentator's objection regarding existence difference.
The objection claims uniform predication fails when concepts differ in existence.
2
Understand the saving interpretation
Notice the phrase 'unless his statement is interpreted as...' which proposes that if essential union is assumed, even hypothetically, predication could be valid.
The example of man and rational animal illustrates this hypothetical union.
3
Reflect on the counter-argument
The passage ends with 'in it there is what there is,' indicating the commentator acknowledges the interpretation has its own issues.
Consider why this hypothetical union might still be problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أن المتغايرين مفهوماً متحدان ذاتاً قال الشارح في حواشي التجريد : يرد عليه أن الأمور المتغايرة في المفهوم إذا تغايرت في الوجود أيضاً، لم يصح حمل بعضهما على بعض بالمواطأة، كما يشهد به البداهة وفيه بحث ظاهر؛ فإن الأمور المتغايرة في الوجود لا يمكن اتحادها بحسب الذات، أي ما صدقت هي عليه، اللهم إلا أن يحمل كلامه على أن الحمل لو في الذات لجاز حمل بعض الأمور المتغابرة في الوجود على بعض، إذا تحقق الاتحاد الذاتي ولو بحسب الفرض أيضاً، كما أن الإنسان لما كان عبارة عن الحيوان الناطق، فإذا تحقق الحيوان الناطق تحقق الإنسان وإن لم تتحقق قابلية العلم الممتنعة الانفكاك عنه، وفيه ما فيه؛ إذ يقال : ما ذكره في حواشي التجريد رد على من قال بتغاير الماهية والجنس والفصل وجوداً ، والاتحاد ذاتاً، أي في الذات التي تركب من اجتماع الأجزاء المتغايرة. قال في حواشي المطالع لا بد في صحة الحمل من الاتحاد في الوجود الخارجي مع التغاير في المفهوم والوجود الذهني، ومنهم من منع ذلك منعاً جدلياً، واكتفى في صحته بالاتحاد في الذوات التي تركبت من اجتماع الأجزاء المتغايرة الوجود في الخارج، ولما لم يكن هذا قادحاً في صحة أصل التعريف بأن تحمل الذات على الما صدق لم يرده في هذا الكتاب. عليه ذات واحدة وجواز صدق المفهومات العدمية على الموجودات الخارجية مما لا شبهة فيه . واعلم أيضاً أن الماهية المركبة من أجزاء خارجية أي غير محمولة عليها، لا قوله : (بمعنى أن ما صدقا عليه ذات واحدة إلخ قيل : الصدق المعدى بعلى معناه الحمل فيلزم الدور . قلت : الحمل معلوم الأنية مجهول الماهية فيجوز أخذه بالوجه الأول في تعريفه بالوجه الثاني وفي قول الشارح قدس سره مما لا شبهة فيه إشارة إلى ما قلنا، وما قال المحقق الدواني من أنه ما لم يتحقق الحمل، لم يتحقق صدق المفهومات المتغايرة على شيء واحد، فإن معنى كون الشيء صادقاً عليه هو كونه متحداً بأنحاء الاتحاد، فتعود شبهة الحمل، فإنك إذا قلت : ( ج و ب ) متحدان فيما صدقا عليه، كان هذا حكماً على شيء واحد بأنه يصدق عليه ( ج و ب ) ، فيقول السائل : إن كان هذا الذات عين كل منهما ، لزم حمل الشيء على نفسه، أو غيره لزم اتحاد الاثنين ولا يحسم مادة الشبهة إلا بأن يقال : هما متحدان في الوجود مختلفان في المفهوم، فمدفوع بأنا لا نسلم الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : إذا قلت ( ج و ب ) متحدان فيما صدقا عليه كان هذا حكماً على شيء واحد بأنه يصدق عليه ( ج وب)، بل كان حكماً بأن تلك الذات جهة اتحادهما .
His saying: (That the two different in concept are united in essence). The commentator said in the margins of al-Tajrīd: There is an objection against it: that things different in concept, if they are also different in existence, the predication of one on the other by uniformity is not valid, as is witnessed by intuition, and in it there is apparent discussion. For things different in existence cannot be united in essence, i.e., what they are true of, unless his statement is interpreted as: predication is [valid] even in essence, so it would be permissible to predicate some things different in existence on others if essential union is verified, even if only by hypothesis, just as man, when he is defined as 'rational animal', if rational animal is verified, then man is verified even if the capacity for knowledge that is inseparable from him is not verified, and in it there is what there is. For it is said: What he mentioned in the margins of al-Tajrīd is a reply to one who said that the essence, genus, and differentia differ in existence but are united in essence, i.e., in the essence that is composed from the gathering of different parts. He said in the margins of al-Maṭāliʿ: For the validity of predication, union in external existence along with difference in concept and mental existence is necessary, and some denied that as a dialectical denial and sufficed for its validity by union in the essences that are composed from the gathering of parts different in existence in the external world, and since this does not invalidate the soundness of the original definition—that the essence is predicated of what it is true of—he did not refute it in this book. Upon it is a single essence, and the permissibility of privative concepts being true of external existents is beyond doubt. Also know that the essence composed of external parts, i.e., not predicated of it, is not. His saying: (Meaning that what they are true of is a single essence etc.) It is said: Truth (ṣidq) modified by 'upon' ('alā) means predication, so a circularity follows. I say: Predication is known in its 'thatness' but unknown in its 'whatness', so it is permissible to take it by the first aspect in its definition by the second aspect. And in the saying of the commentator (may his soul be sanctified) 'beyond doubt' there is an indication to what we said. And what al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī said—that unless predication is verified, the truth of different concepts of one thing is not verified, for the meaning of a thing being true of it is its being united by ways of union—so the difficulty of predication returns. For if you say: 'J and B are united in what they are true of', this is a judgment on one thing that 'J and B' are true of it. So the questioner says: If this essence is identical to each of them, then predication of a thing on itself follows; if it is other, then the union of two things follows. And the difficulty is not cut off except by saying: They are united in existence, different in concept. It is refuted by: We do not accept the implication derived from his saying: 'If you say J and B are united in what they are true of, this is a judgment on one thing that J and B are true of it', rather it is a judgment that that essence is the aspect of their union.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. The commentator quotes the definition of predication as 'two different concepts united in essence' and presents a criticism: if concepts are different and also different in existence, they cannot be predicated uniformly. However, if we interpret the definition to allow hypothetical essential union, then predication is possible, as in man being rational animal. The commentator then discusses various views on the conditions of predication: some require union in external existence, others suffice with union in the composed essence. The passage also addresses an objection that the definition leads to circularity because 'truth' (ṣidq) already implies predication. The reply is that predication is known by its 'thatness' but not its 'whatness', so using it in the definition is permissible. Finally, al-Dawwānī's argument that predication reduces to union is refuted by showing that saying 'J and B are united in what they are true of' is not a judgment on one thing but a judgment on the essence as the aspect of union.

C) They must be different in both concept and existence.
D) Predication is never possible if concepts differ in existence.

Key Takeaway
The definition of predication as two different concepts united in essence faces the objection that existence differences prevent predication, but a hypothetical essential union can salvage it.

This lesson shows that even in logic, apparent contradictions can be overcome by hypothetical union. ?

The example of man and rational animal shows that essential union can be recognized even when existence differs. Conditions for Predication and the Circularity Objection
Conditions for predication and resolution of circularity and al-Dawwānī's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different views on the conditions for predication (union in external existence vs. union in composed essence) and how the circularity objection is resolved by distinguishing the 'thatness' and 'whatness' of predication.

Core Idea
Predication requires union, but the type of union is debated; circularity is avoided by definition from a known aspect.

This lesson continues the discussion on predication by presenting two views: one requiring union in external existence, another requiring only union in the composed essence. It then addresses an objection that the definition of predication is circular because it uses 'truth' which already implies predication. The commentator replies that predication is known in its 'thatness' but not its 'whatness', allowing it to be used in a definition.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two views on union
Read the passage starting from 'He said in the margins of al-Maṭāliʿ'. Compare the view that requires union in external existence with the view that suffices with union in the composed essence.
Note that the commentator notes the second view does not invalidate the original definition.
2
Understand the circularity objection
Identify the argument that using 'truth' (ṣidq) in the definition leads to circularity because 'truth' itself means predication.
The objection claims the definition defines predication by something that presupposes it.
3
Grasp the reply about thatness and whatness
The commentator says predication is known in its 'thatness' (that it is) but not its 'whatness' (what it is essentially), so it can be taken by its first aspect in defining its second aspect.
This is a key logical move to avoid circularity.
4
Consider al-Dawwānī's argument
Read al-Dawwānī's point that predication reduces to union, leading to a regress. The commentator refutes it by saying the statement 'J and B are united in what they are true of' is not a judgment on one thing but on the essence as the aspect of union.
This resolves the difficulty by distinguishing between a judgment about a thing and a judgment about an aspect.

صحة أصل التعريف بأن تحمل الذات على الما صدق لم يرده في هذا الكتاب. عليه ذات واحدة وجواز صدق المفهومات العدمية على الموجودات الخارجية مما لا شبهة فيه . واعلم أيضاً أن الماهية المركبة من أجزاء خارجية أي غير محمولة عليها، لا قوله : (بمعنى أن ما صدقا عليه ذات واحدة إلخ قيل : الصدق المعدى بعلى معناه الحمل فيلزم الدور . قلت : الحمل معلوم الأنية مجهول الماهية فيجوز أخذه بالوجه الأول في تعريفه بالوجه الثاني وفي قول الشارح قدس سره مما لا شبهة فيه إشارة إلى ما قلنا، وما قال المحقق الدواني من أنه ما لم يتحقق الحمل، لم يتحقق صدق المفهومات المتغايرة على شيء واحد، فإن معنى كون الشيء صادقاً عليه هو كونه متحداً بأنحاء الاتحاد، فتعود شبهة الحمل، فإنك إذا قلت : ( ج و ب ) متحدان فيما صدقا عليه، كان هذا حكماً على شيء واحد بأنه يصدق عليه ( ج و ب ) ، فيقول السائل : إن كان هذا الذات عين كل منهما ، لزم حمل الشيء على نفسه، أو غيره لزم اتحاد الاثنين ولا يحسم مادة الشبهة إلا بأن يقال : هما متحدان في الوجود مختلفان في المفهوم، فمدفوع بأنا لا نسلم الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : إذا قلت ( ج و ب ) متحدان فيما صدقا عليه كان هذا حكماً على شيء واحد بأنه يصدق عليه ( ج وب)، بل كان حكماً بأن تلك الذات جهة اتحادهما .
His saying: (That the two different in concept are united in essence). The commentator said in the margins of al-Tajrīd: There is an objection against it: that things different in concept, if they are also different in existence, the predication of one on the other by uniformity is not valid, as is witnessed by intuition, and in it there is apparent discussion. For things different in existence cannot be united in essence, i.e., what they are true of, unless his statement is interpreted as: predication is [valid] even in essence, so it would be permissible to predicate some things different in existence on others if essential union is verified, even if only by hypothesis, just as man, when he is defined as 'rational animal', if rational animal is verified, then man is verified even if the capacity for knowledge that is inseparable from him is not verified, and in it there is what there is. For it is said: What he mentioned in the margins of al-Tajrīd is a reply to one who said that the essence, genus, and differentia differ in existence but are united in essence, i.e., in the essence that is composed from the gathering of different parts. He said in the margins of al-Maṭāliʿ: For the validity of predication, union in external existence along with difference in concept and mental existence is necessary, and some denied that as a dialectical denial and sufficed for its validity by union in the essences that are composed from the gathering of parts different in existence in the external world, and since this does not invalidate the soundness of the original definition—that the essence is predicated of what it is true of—he did not refute it in this book. Upon it is a single essence, and the permissibility of privative concepts being true of external existents is beyond doubt. Also know that the essence composed of external parts, i.e., not predicated of it, is not. His saying: (Meaning that what they are true of is a single essence etc.) It is said: Truth (ṣidq) modified by 'upon' ('alā) means predication, so a circularity follows. I say: Predication is known in its 'thatness' but unknown in its 'whatness', so it is permissible to take it by the first aspect in its definition by the second aspect. And in the saying of the commentator (may his soul be sanctified) 'beyond doubt' there is an indication to what we said. And what al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī said—that unless predication is verified, the truth of different concepts of one thing is not verified, for the meaning of a thing being true of it is its being united by ways of union—so the difficulty of predication returns. For if you say: 'J and B are united in what they are true of', this is a judgment on one thing that 'J and B' are true of it. So the questioner says: If this essence is identical to each of them, then predication of a thing on itself follows; if it is other, then the union of two things follows. And the difficulty is not cut off except by saying: They are united in existence, different in concept. It is refuted by: We do not accept the implication derived from his saying: 'If you say J and B are united in what they are true of, this is a judgment on one thing that J and B are true of it', rather it is a judgment that that essence is the aspect of their union.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. The commentator quotes the definition of predication as 'two different concepts united in essence' and presents a criticism: if concepts are different and also different in existence, they cannot be predicated uniformly. However, if we interpret the definition to allow hypothetical essential union, then predication is possible, as in man being rational animal. The commentator then discusses various views on the conditions of predication: some require union in external existence, others suffice with union in the composed essence. The passage also addresses an objection that the definition leads to circularity because 'truth' (ṣidq) already implies predication. The reply is that predication is known by its 'thatness' but not its 'whatness', so using it in the definition is permissible. Finally, al-Dawwānī's argument that predication reduces to union is refuted by showing that saying 'J and B are united in what they are true of' is not a judgment on one thing but a judgment on the essence as the aspect of union.

Misconception: The definition of predication is circular because it uses 'truth' which implies predication.
Correction: The commentator argues that predication is known in its thatness (its occurrence) but not its whatness (its essence), so using it in a definition is permissible as a definition by a known aspect.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator avoid the circularity objection in defining predication?
A) He denies that 'truth' means predication.
B) He says predication is a simple concept that cannot be defined.
C) He distinguishes the 'thatness' from the 'whatness' of predication.
D) He uses a different definition altogether.

Key Takeaway
The circularity objection to defining predication is resolved by distinguishing the known occurrence (thatness) from the unknown essence (whatness), allowing a non-circular definition.

Two Objections to the Definition of Predication as Union in Essence
Two objections to the definition of predication as union in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main logical objections to the definition of predication as 'union in essence' and why they target its applicability and circularity.

Core Idea
The definition of predication as union in essence fails for accidental predications and risks circularity.

After examining the definition of predication as two concepts united in essence, the author now presents two critical objections. The first points out a potential circularity in using 'truth' (ṣidq) to define predication, while the second argues that the definition does not hold for propositions like 'Zaid is standing.' These objections are central to understanding the precise requirements for a sound definition of predication in logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the two objections presented.
The passage is truncated at the end, but the two objections are clear.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection concerns circularity: the definition uses 'truth' (ṣidq) which itself means predication. Consider why this is problematic.
Unless it is a verbal definition, this seems circular.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection states that the definition does not apply to accidental predications like 'Zaid is standing'. Reflect on why the subject and predicate are not united in essence here.
Zaid and 'standing' are not the same essence.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Do these objections completely invalidate the definition, or can they be answered? The author notes a possible escape for the first objection (verbal definition).
Consider how a defender might respond.

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of objections to the definition of predication as 'two different concepts united in essence'. It first discusses a related issue: the example of man as rational animal, where even if certain inseparable attributes are not verified, the definition holds. Then it presents two direct objections to the definition: (1) The term 'truth' (ṣidq) in the definition already means predication, leading to circularity—unless it is a verbal definition. (2) The definition fails for accidental predications like 'Zaid is standing', because the subject (Zaid) does not have a single essence with the predicate (standing). The second objection is that the definition does not apply to such true but accidental predications.

Predication, Definition, and the Circularity Objection
Commentary on predication definition and refutation of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining predication via applicability of concepts is not circular and how the distinction between thatness and quiddity resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Predication is known in its thatness but unknown in its quiddity — that breaks the circularity.

This passage delves into a classic objection against defining predication (ḥaml) as 'two concepts applying to a single essence.' The objection claims circularity because the word for 'applicability' (ṣidq) itself means predication. But the commentator argues that predication is known in its thatness (anniyya) — we know it exists — while its quiddity is unknown; thus we can use it informally in the definition. The passage then engages with a further argument by al-Dawānī and refutes it. Let's unpack this subtle reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main objection
Read the passage and locate the objection: that using ṣidq (applicability) to define ḥaml (predication) leads to circularity. Note the response: distinction between āniyya (thatness) and māhiyya (quiddity).
2
Examine al-Dawānī's counter-argument
Al-Dawānī argues that without knowing predication, we cannot even understand two concepts applying to one thing. The commentator shows how this leads to a regress.
3
Understand the final refutation
The proposed solution (united in existence, different in concept) is rejected because the implication that saying 'J and B are united in what they apply to' means there is a single thing they apply to is not conceded. Instead, it means that essence is the locus of union.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بمعنى أن ما صدقا عليه ذات واحدة إلخ قيل : الصدق المعدى بعلى معناه الحمل فيلزم الدور . قلت : الحمل معلوم الأنية مجهول الماهية فيجوز أخذه بالوجه الأول في تعريفه بالوجه الثاني وفي قول الشارح قدس سره مما لا شبهة فيه إشارة إلى ما قلنا، وما قال المحقق الدواني من أنه ما لم يتحقق الحمل، لم يتحقق صدق المفهومات المتغايرة على شيء واحد، فإن معنى كون الشيء صادقاً عليه هو كونه متحداً بأنحاء الاتحاد، فتعود شبهة الحمل، فإنك إذا قلت : ( ج و ب ) متحدان فيما صدقا عليه، كان هذا حكماً على شيء واحد بأنه يصدق عليه ( ج و ب ) ، فيقول السائل : إن كان هذا الذات عين كل منهما ، لزم حمل الشيء على نفسه، أو غيره لزم اتحاد الاثنين ولا يحسم مادة الشبهة إلا بأن يقال : هما متحدان في الوجود مختلفان في المفهوم، فمدفوع بأنا لا نسلم الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : إذا قلت ( ج و ب ) متحدان فيما صدقا عليه كان هذا حكماً على شيء واحد بأنه يصدق عليه ( ج وب)، بل كان حكماً بأن تلك الذات جهة اتحادهما .
His saying: (Meaning that what both [concepts] apply to is a single essence etc.) It was said: Predication (ṣidq) with 'alā means predication (ḥaml), so circularity follows. I say: Predication is known in its thatness (anniyya) but unknown in its quiddity (māhiyya), so it is permissible to take it in the first aspect in its definition by the second aspect. And in the saying of the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) 'there is no doubt about it' is an indication of what we said. And what al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawānī said—that as long as predication is not verified, the applicability (ṣidq) of differing concepts to one thing is not verified—for the meaning of a thing's being true of (ṣādiq ʿalā) something is that it is united in ways of union, so the suspicion of predication returns. For if you say: 'J and B are united in what they apply to,' this is a judgment about a single thing that J and B apply to it. Then the questioner says: If this essence is identical to each of them, then it would be predication of a thing on itself; or if it is other, then it would necessitate the union of the two. And the source of suspicion is not resolved except by saying: they are united in existence, different in concept. This is refuted by our not conceding the implication derived from his saying: 'When you say J and B are united in what they apply to, this is a judgment about a single thing that J and B apply to it'; rather, it was a judgment that that essence is the locus of their union.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the definition of predication (ḥaml) and a refutation of the circularity objection. The commentator first states an objection: if predication (ṣidq with ʿalā) means predication itself, then defining predication this way leads to circularity. The response is that predication is known in its 'thatness' (anniyya) as a fact, but its quiddity is unknown, so it can be taken as an undefined notion in the first step and then defined in a second step. The commentator then engages with al-Dawānī's argument that predication is necessary for different concepts to apply to the same thing, and this leads to a regress. The example of two concepts J and B being 'united in what they apply to' is examined. The questioner argues that if the essence is identical to each, it is self-predication; if different, then the two become one. The proposed solution—they are united in existence but different in concept—is rejected because the implication that saying 'J and B are united in what they apply to' means there is a single thing they apply to is not conceded; rather, it means that essence is the locus of their union.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining predication via applicability (ṣidq) is circular because ṣidq means predication itself.
Correction: It is not circular because we know predication exists (thatness) even if we don't know its essence (quiddity); thus we can use the concept informally in the definition.

Checkpoint

C) It only applies to non-material essences.
D) It is too narrow.

Key Takeaway
Defining predication via applicability of concepts to a single essence is not circular if we distinguish between the known fact of predication (thatness) and its unknown essence (quiddity).
