Three Views on External vs. Mental Composition
Enumeration of three positions on the relation between external and mental composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three historical positions on the relationship between external and mental composition and how each affects the definition of an essence.

Core Idea
Three ways to relate external parts to mental parts: exclusive, inclusive, or identical.

In this passage, the commentator presents three competing views on how external composition (the actual parts of a thing) relates to mental composition (the parts we use in definition). This debate directly affects what counts as a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm) versus a mere description (rasm). As you read, notice how each view yields different consequences for defining simple versus composite entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three views
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the three positions: mutual exclusivity (mutabāyin), generality (aʿamm), and equality (musāwī).
The key terms mutabāyin, aʿamm, musāwī are crucial.
2
Understand the ontological stakes
Each view makes a claim about whether mental composition mirrors external composition or not. Reflect on how this affects definitions.
3
Connect each view to its proponent
Match each position to its scholar: Ṣāḥib al-Muḥākamāt (exclusivity), the majority and Ibn Sīnā (generality), and al-Dawānī (equality).
This helps contextualize the debate historically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( واعلم إلخ) ما مر كان بياناً لتركب الماهية من الأجزاء المحمولة، وهذا بيان النسبة بين التركيبين، وفيها أيضاً ثلاثة مذاهب ووجه الضبط أن التركيب الخارجي إما أن يكون مبايناً للتركيب الذهني حتى إن كل مركب خارجي لا يجوز تركيبه من الأجزاء المحمولة، فالحد التام له إنما هو بالأجزاء الخارجية والتعريفات بالأجزاء المحمولة كلها رسوم وإليه ذهب صاحب المحاكمات، واختاره الشارح قدس سره، أو لا يكون مبايناً له، فإما أن يكون التركيب الذهني أعم منه، فكل مركب خارجي مركب ذهني ولا عكس كما في الحقائق البسيطة. وإليه ذهب الجمهور، وهو مختار الشيخ في الشفاء، أو يكون التركيب الذهني مساويا للتركيب الخارجي، واختاره المحقق الدواني وقال : إن التركيب الذهني مختص بالمركبات الخارجية، والبسائط لا تركيب فيها حقيقة، وإنما يؤخذ الجنس والفصل منها بضرب من التحليل .
His saying: (And know etc.) What preceded was an explanation of the composition of the essence from predicated parts, and this is an explanation of the relationship between the two compositions. In it also are three schools. The way to grasp it is that external composition is either mutually exclusive (mutabāyin) with mental composition, such that no externally composite thing can be composed of predicated parts; therefore its complete definition (ḥadd tāmm) is only by external parts, and definitions by predicated parts are all descriptions (rusūm). This is the view of Ṣāḥib al-Muḥākamāt, and the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) chose it. Or it is not mutually exclusive; then either mental composition is more general (aʿamm) than it, so every externally composite is mentally composite but not conversely, as in simple realities (al-ḥaqāʾiq al-basīṭa). This is the view of the majority (al-jumhūr), and it is the choice of the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) in al-Shifāʾ. Or mental composition is equal (musāwī) to external composition; al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawānī chose this and said: mental composition is exclusive to external composites, and simple things have no composition in reality, but genus and difference are taken from them by a kind of analysis (taḥlīl).
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of three positions on the relationship between external composition (tarkīb khārijī) and mental composition (tarkīb dhihnī). The commentator first notes that the earlier discussion explained the composition of the essence from predicated parts, and now he explains the relationship between the two types of composition. The first view: external and mental composition are mutually exclusive (mutabāyin). On this view, nothing externally composite can be composed of predicated parts. Consequently, its complete definition (ḥadd tāmm) is given solely by external parts, and any definition using predicated parts counts only as a description (rasm). This is held by Ṣāḥib al-Muḥākamāt and adopted by the commentator. The second view: mental composition is more general (aʿamm) than external composition. Every externally composite is mentally composite, but not every mentally composite is externally composite, as in simple realities (ḥaqāʾiq basīṭa) which have mental but not external composition. This is the view of the majority and is chosen by Ibn Sīnā in al-Shifāʾ. The third view: mental composition is equal (musāwī) to external composition. Al-Dawānī chooses this: mental composition is exclusive to external composites; simple essences have no composition at all—genus and difference are extracted from them only by analysis (taḥlīl), not as real components.

Key Terms
تركيب خارجي (tarkīb khārijī)
تركيب ذهني (tarkīb dhihnī)
حد تام (ḥadd tāmm)
رسم (rasm)
مباين (mutabāyin)
أعم (aʿamm)
مساو (musāwī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three views are merely about definitional methods.
Correction: They are about the ontological relationship between external and mental composition, which then affects definition.

Checkpoint
Which scholar held that mental composition is equal to external composition and that simple essences have no real composition?
A) Ṣāḥib al-Muḥākamāt
B) The commentator (al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī)
C) Al-Ṭūsī
D) Al-Dawānī

Key Takeaway
The three positions on external vs. mental composition show how different metaphysical commitments lead to different theories of definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define something in your life—like 'justice' or 'success'—do you tend to rely on external, observable parts, or mental concepts? This lesson shows that your approach reflects a metaphysical commitment. Examine one definition you hold. Does it match reality or is it purely mental? What would change if you adopted a different view? Take a moment to write down a commitment to align your definitions with truth.
?
The commentator chose the view that external and mental composition are mutually exclusive. Do you agree? How does your own view affect your understanding of the essence of God (who is absolutely simple)? If you hold that mental composition cannot capture external reality, then how do you trust your concepts of God? Ponder this and decide: will you humbly accept the limits of your definitions or strive to refine them? Commit to seeking clarity in your understanding of divine attributes.

The Impossibility of Dual Composition
Core argument against dual composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a composite cannot have both external parts (أجزاء خارجية) and logical parts (أجزاء محمولة) without leading to two distinct realities for one thing.

Core Idea
External parts alone define a composite in the mind; adding logical parts creates a second reality.

This lesson presents the core argument of the commentator: the total of external parts already constitutes the complete quiddity of a composite in the mind. If there were distinct mental parts, that sum would also be the complete quiddity, resulting in two different realities for the same thing. This foundational argument sets the stage for further discussion of composite entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the concise logical style.
The passage uses technical terms like 'أجزاء خارجية' and 'أجزاء محمولة'.
2
Identify the key premise
The author states that the sum of external parts is the complete reality of the composite in both mind and external. Locate this statement and understand its significance.
3
Understand the contradiction
If there were additional mental parts, their sum would also be a complete reality, leading to two different realities for one thing. Grasp why this is impossible.
The argument hinges on the unity of the quiddity.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this rules out a composite having both external and logical parts simultaneously. Connect to the broader topic of composition.
This argument is foundational for later discussions on genus and differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد العاشر : وجوب حاجة بعض الأجزاء إلى بعض الملتئمة من على مجموع تلك الأجزاء حداً تاماً لها، إذ لا معنى للتحديد التام إلا تصوير كنه الماهية، فلو كان لها أجزاء محمولة أيضا، فإن لم تشتمل على تلك الأجزاء لم تحصل منها صورة مطابقة للماهية المفروضة ؛ لأن الصورة المطابقة لها هي تلك الأجزاء، وإن اشتملت عليها فحينئذ إن لم تشتمل على أمر زائد، كانت هي تلك الأجزاء بعينها، لا أجزاء محمولة، وإن اشتملت على أمر زائد فذلك الزائد إن دخل في الماهية كانت حقيقتها قابلة للزيادة والنقصان وإن لم تدخل فلا اعتبار به في الأجزاء. وبالجملة : مجموع الأجزاء الخارجية تمام حقيقة المركب في العقل كما أنه تمام حقيقته في الخارج، فلو كان له أجزاء عقلية مغايرة لتلك الأجزاء، لكان مجموعها أيضاً تمام ماهية المركب في العقل، فيلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان
قوله : ( لأن الصورة المطابقة لها هي الملتئمة إلخ ) يعني أن المطابقة منحصرة في الملتئمة الأجزاء الغير المحمولة؛ إذ لا فرق بينها وبين الماهية إلا بالإجمال والتفصيل، والمفروض أن الصورة الملتئمة من الأجزاء المحمولة مخالفة للصورة المذكورة، فلا تكون تلك الصورة مطابقة للماهية لامتناع مطابقة أمرين متخالفين لأمر واحد، بأن يكون كل منهما صورة تمام الماهية. قوله : كانت هي تلك الأجزاء بعينها لا أجزاء محمولة فيه بحث؛ لأن الأجزاء المحمولة الأجزاء الخارجية ذاتاً، والفرق بينهما باعتبار أخذ المحمولة لا بشرط، والخارجية بشرط لا، وهو مناط الحمل وعدمه كما عرفت .
قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ) أي نترك التفصيل المذكور ونقول مجملاً هكذا.
قوله : ( مغايرة لتلك الأجزاء) بالذات إما كلاً أو بعضاً.
قوله : ( فيلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد إلخ) قد عرفت أنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو لم تتحد الأجزاء المحمولة والخارجية بالذات .
العينية وجودات متمايزة في الخارج، ومعنى كون المركب الخارجي مركباً عقلياً ذا أجزاء عقلية أن يكون للأجزاء العينية وجودات متمايزة ،فيه فيختار أن الأجزاء المحمولة، بعينها هي الخارجية بلا شامل ومشمول، وإنما التمايز بعارض الوجود وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في تركب المركب الخارجي من الأجزاء المحمولة وأن الصور العقلية على هذا التصوير لا تحمل على الكل . قوله : ( فيلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان أي تماما حقيقتين مختلفين، كما ظهر من تقريره، فلايرد تجويزنا مطابقة كل من الجنس والفصل والنوع لزيد مثلاً . وقد يقال : نعم لزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان، لكن إحداهما حقيقية خارجية، والأخرى ذهنية، وقد لا نسلم امتناعه وأنت خبير بأنه لزم من التصوير المذكور أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان ذهنيتان؛ لأن مجموع الأجزاء الخارجية تمام حقيقة المركب في العقل، كما أنه تمام حقيقته في الخارج على ما صرح به اللهم إلا أن يقال : الأجزاء الخارجية لجذائها ( هكذا) لا مختلفة في العقل، وأنه محال فبطل ما قيل من أن تركب الماهية من أجزاء غير محمولة لا ينافي تركبها من أجزاء محمولة، بل كل مركب خارجي إذا اشتق من جزئه المشترك بينه وبين غيره، كان ذلك المشتق جنساً له، وإذا اشتق من جزئه المختص به كان فصلاً له، وكل مركب فإنه مركب من الجنس والفصل، وكيف لا يبطل، والاشتقاق يخرج الجزء عن الجزئية؛ إذ لا بد أن يعتبر الجزء مع نسبة هي خارجة عن ماهية المركب، فإن النسبة بين الجزء والكل خارجة عنهما قطعاً، والجزء المأخوذ مع الخارج خارج. وتحقق عندك أن المركب من أجزاء غير محمولة لا يجوز أن يتركب من أجزاء محمولة وأن المركب من الأجزاء المحمولة لا يكون إلا بسيطاً في الخارج.
The second observatory – the tenth purpose: The necessity of some parts needing others. ... composed from the total of those parts as a complete definition for it, since there is no meaning of complete definition except depicting the essence of the quiddity. So if it also has predicative parts, then if it does not include those parts, a form matching the assumed quiddity is not obtained from them; because the matching form is those parts. And if it includes them, then if it does not include an additional matter, they are those very parts, not predicative parts. And if it includes an additional matter, then if that additional enters into the quiddity, its reality becomes susceptible to increase and decrease; and if it does not enter, then it is disregarded in the parts. In sum: The total of the external parts is the complete reality of the composite in the mind, just as it is its complete reality in the external. So if it had mental parts distinct from those parts, then their total would also be the complete quiddity of the composite in the mind, so one thing would have two realities. His statement: 'Because the matching form is the composed one, etc.' means that congruence is restricted to the form composed of non-predicative parts; since there is no difference between it and the quiddity except by generality and detail, and it is assumed that the form composed of predicative parts differs from the mentioned form, so that form cannot match the quiddity due to the impossibility of two differing things matching one thing, such that each of them is the complete form of the quiddity. His statement: 'They are those very parts, not predicative parts' – there is discussion in it, because predicative parts are the external parts in essence, and the difference between them is based on taking the predicative without condition and the external with the condition of not being predicated, which is the criterion of predication and its absence as you know. His statement: 'And in sum, etc.' – that is, we leave the detailed discussion and say summarily like this. His statement: 'Distinct from those parts' – in essence, either wholly or partially. His statement: 'Then one thing would have two realities, etc.' – you know that this follows only if the predicative and external parts are not identical in essence. The essential parts have distinct existences in the external, and the meaning of the external composite being a mental composite with mental parts is that the essential parts have distinct existences, so it is chosen that the predicative parts are exactly the external parts, not inclusive and included, but the distinction is by an accident of existence. And you are aware that the discussion is about the composition of the external composite from predicative parts, and that mental forms in this depiction are not predicated of the whole. His statement: 'Then one thing would have two different realities' – that is, two complete different realities, as appears from his exposition. So our allowing that each of genus, differentia, and species matches Zayd, for example, does not apply. And it may be said: Yes, it follows that one thing has two different realities, but one is an external reality and the other is a mental reality, and we may not concede its impossibility. And you are aware that from the mentioned depiction it follows that one thing has two different mental realities, because the total of the external parts is the complete reality of the composite in the mind, just as it is its complete reality in the external as he explicitly stated. Unless it is said: The external parts ... are not different in the mind, and that is impossible. So what was said, that the composition of a quiddity from non-predicative parts does not contradict its composition from predicative parts, is false. Rather, every external composite, if it is derived from its part common between it and something else, then that derivative is its genus; and if it is derived from its specific part, then it is its differentia; and every composite is composed of genus and differentia. And how could it not be false, while derivation removes the part from part-hood; because the part must be considered with a relation that is external to the quiddity of the composite, so the part taken with the external is external. And it has been verified for you that a composite of non-predicative parts cannot be composed of predicative parts, and that the composite of predicative parts is only simple in the external.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained argument against the possibility of a composite having both external parts (أجزاء خارجية) and logical parts (أجزاء محمولة, such as genus and species) in the mind. The author begins by stating that the total of external parts constitutes the complete definition of the composite both externally and mentally. If there were additional mental parts distinct from these, then that sum would also be the complete definition, leading to two different realities for one thing. The commentator then glosses this argument, explaining that the matching mental form is specifically the one composed of non-predicative parts. He addresses an objection: if the composite includes an extra element, it either enters the quiddity (making it subject to increase) or does not (making it irrelevant). He then focuses on the premise that if the external parts are the complete reality in the mind, then any other mental parts would be a second reality. An objector suggests that one reality could be external and the other mental, but the reply is that the external parts themselves constitute the mental reality, resulting in two mental realities. The commentator further refutes the claim that a quiddity can be composed of both types of parts by arguing that derivation (اشتقاق) removes a part from part-hood because it requires adding an external relation. Therefore, a composite of external parts cannot also be composed of logical parts, and a composite of logical parts is simple in the external. The argument concludes that the two types of composition are mutually exclusive.

Key Terms
أجزاء خارجية
أجزاء محمولة
ماهية
حقيقة
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a composite can have both external parts and logical parts as long as they are considered from different perspectives.
Correction: The argument shows that if logical parts are distinct, they constitute a separate complete reality, violating the unity of the quiddity.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why can't a composite have both external and logical parts?
A) Because logical parts are not real.
B) Because the sum of external parts is already the complete mental reality, so additional mental parts would create a second reality.
C) Because external parts cannot be predicated of the whole.
D) Because derivation always removes part-hood.

Key Takeaway
The total of external parts fully defines a composite in the mind, so any additional mental parts would create a second distinct reality, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own self-understanding, do you tend to see yourself as a composite of external features (body, actions) plus an inner 'extra' (soul, personality) that is entirely separate? How does the argument that this would create two realities challenge your view of your own unity?
?
This lesson concludes that one thing cannot have two complete realities. Has there ever been a time when you felt pulled between two different 'selves'—perhaps a private self and a public self? How does this logical principle call you to seek integrity and oneness of your being before Allah?

Objections and Clarifications on Dual Composition
Refutation of objections and final conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections to the main argument and the commentator's responses, including the role of derivation in excluding logical parts.

Core Idea
Logical parts are not separate entities; they are the external parts considered without restriction.

This lesson continues from the previous one, addressing objections and clarifications. The commentator responds to the suggestion that one reality could be external and the other mental, and explains that derivation (اشتقاق) removes a part from part-hood by adding an external relation. This reinforces the conclusion that a composite cannot be composed of both types of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The objector says that one reality could be external and the other mental. The commentator replies that the external parts themselves constitute the mental reality, so both would be mental. Read carefully.
2
Understand the point about derivation
The commentator argues that derivation removes a part from part-hood because it requires an external relation. Grasp how this refutes the idea of logical parts.
Derivation produces genus and differentia, but these are not additional parts.
3
Connect to the overall argument
See how these clarifications support the main argument that external and logical parts cannot coexist in one composite.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final statement: a composite of external parts cannot be composed of logical parts, and a composite of logical parts is simple in the external. Understand why.

Source Text

المرصد الثاني - المقصد العاشر : وجوب حاجة بعض الأجزاء إلى بعض الملتئمة من على مجموع تلك الأجزاء حداً تاماً لها، إذ لا معنى للتحديد التام إلا تصوير كنه الماهية، فلو كان لها أجزاء محمولة أيضا، فإن لم تشتمل على تلك الأجزاء لم تحصل منها صورة مطابقة للماهية المفروضة ؛ لأن الصورة المطابقة لها هي تلك الأجزاء، وإن اشتملت عليها فحينئذ إن لم تشتمل على أمر زائد، كانت هي تلك الأجزاء بعينها، لا أجزاء محمولة، وإن اشتملت على أمر زائد فذلك الزائد إن دخل في الماهية كانت حقيقتها قابلة للزيادة والنقصان وإن لم تدخل فلا اعتبار به في الأجزاء. وبالجملة : مجموع الأجزاء الخارجية تمام حقيقة المركب في العقل كما أنه تمام حقيقته في الخارج، فلو كان له أجزاء عقلية مغايرة لتلك الأجزاء، لكان مجموعها أيضاً تمام ماهية المركب في العقل، فيلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان

قوله : ( مغايرة لتلك الأجزاء) بالذات إما كلاً أو بعضاً.

العينية وجودات متمايزة في الخارج، ومعنى كون المركب الخارجي مركباً عقلياً ذا أجزاء عقلية أن يكون للأجزاء العينية وجودات متمايزة ،فيه فيختار أن الأجزاء المحمولة، بعينها هي الخارجية بلا شامل ومشمول، وإنما التمايز بعارض الوجود وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في تركب المركب الخارجي من الأجزاء المحمولة وأن الصور العقلية على هذا التصوير لا تحمل على الكل . قوله : ( فيلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان أي تماما حقيقتين مختلفين، كما ظهر من تقريره، فلايرد تجويزنا مطابقة كل من الجنس والفصل والنوع لزيد مثلاً . وقد يقال : نعم لزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان، لكن إحداهما حقيقية خارجية، والأخرى ذهنية، وقد لا نسلم امتناعه وأنت خبير بأنه لزم من التصوير المذكور أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان ذهنيتان؛ لأن مجموع الأجزاء الخارجية تمام حقيقة المركب في العقل، كما أنه تمام حقيقته في الخارج على ما صرح به اللهم إلا أن يقال : الأجزاء الخارجية لجذائها ( هكذا) لا مختلفة في العقل، وأنه محال فبطل ما قيل من أن تركب الماهية من أجزاء غير محمولة لا ينافي تركبها من أجزاء محمولة، بل كل مركب خارجي إذا اشتق من جزئه المشترك بينه وبين غيره، كان ذلك المشتق جنساً له، وإذا اشتق من جزئه المختص به كان فصلاً له، وكل مركب فإنه مركب من الجنس والفصل، وكيف لا يبطل، والاشتقاق يخرج الجزء عن الجزئية؛ إذ لا بد أن يعتبر الجزء مع نسبة هي خارجة عن ماهية المركب، فإن النسبة بين الجزء والكل خارجة عنهما قطعاً، والجزء المأخوذ مع الخارج خارج. وتحقق عندك أن المركب من أجزاء غير محمولة لا يجوز أن يتركب من أجزاء محمولة وأن المركب من الأجزاء المحمولة لا يكون إلا بسيطاً في الخارج.
The second observatory – the tenth purpose: The necessity of some parts needing others. ... composed from the total of those parts as a complete definition for it, since there is no meaning of complete definition except depicting the essence of the quiddity. So if it also has predicative parts, then if it does not include those parts, a form matching the assumed quiddity is not obtained from them; because the matching form is those parts. And if it includes them, then if it does not include an additional matter, they are those very parts, not predicative parts. And if it includes an additional matter, then if that additional enters into the quiddity, its reality becomes susceptible to increase and decrease; and if it does not enter, then it is disregarded in the parts. In sum: The total of the external parts is the complete reality of the composite in the mind, just as it is its complete reality in the external. So if it had mental parts distinct from those parts, then their total would also be the complete quiddity of the composite in the mind, so one thing would have two realities. His statement: 'Because the matching form is the composed one, etc.' means that congruence is restricted to the form composed of non-predicative parts; since there is no difference between it and the quiddity except by generality and detail, and it is assumed that the form composed of predicative parts differs from the mentioned form, so that form cannot match the quiddity due to the impossibility of two differing things matching one thing, such that each of them is the complete form of the quiddity. His statement: 'They are those very parts, not predicative parts' – there is discussion in it, because predicative parts are the external parts in essence, and the difference between them is based on taking the predicative without condition and the external with the condition of not being predicated, which is the criterion of predication and its absence as you know. His statement: 'And in sum, etc.' – that is, we leave the detailed discussion and say summarily like this. His statement: 'Distinct from those parts' – in essence, either wholly or partially. His statement: 'Then one thing would have two realities, etc.' – you know that this follows only if the predicative and external parts are not identical in essence. The essential parts have distinct existences in the external, and the meaning of the external composite being a mental composite with mental parts is that the essential parts have distinct existences, so it is chosen that the predicative parts are exactly the external parts, not inclusive and included, but the distinction is by an accident of existence. And you are aware that the discussion is about the composition of the external composite from predicative parts, and that mental forms in this depiction are not predicated of the whole. His statement: 'Then one thing would have two different realities' – that is, two complete different realities, as appears from his exposition. So our allowing that each of genus, differentia, and species matches Zayd, for example, does not apply. And it may be said: Yes, it follows that one thing has two different realities, but one is an external reality and the other is a mental reality, and we may not concede its impossibility. And you are aware that from the mentioned depiction it follows that one thing has two different mental realities, because the total of the external parts is the complete reality of the composite in the mind, just as it is its complete reality in the external as he explicitly stated. Unless it is said: The external parts ... are not different in the mind, and that is impossible. So what was said, that the composition of a quiddity from non-predicative parts does not contradict its composition from predicative parts, is false. Rather, every external composite, if it is derived from its part common between it and something else, then that derivative is its genus; and if it is derived from its specific part, then it is its differentia; and every composite is composed of genus and differentia. And how could it not be false, while derivation removes the part from part-hood; because the part must be considered with a relation that is external to the quiddity of the composite, so the part taken with the external is external. And it has been verified for you that a composite of non-predicative parts cannot be composed of predicative parts, and that the composite of predicative parts is only simple in the external.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained argument against the possibility of a composite having both external parts (أجزاء خارجية) and logical parts (أجزاء محمولة, such as genus and species) in the mind. The author begins by stating that the total of external parts constitutes the complete definition of the composite both externally and mentally. If there were additional mental parts distinct from these, then that sum would also be the complete definition, leading to two different realities for one thing. The commentator then glosses this argument, explaining that the matching mental form is specifically the one composed of non-predicative parts. He addresses an objection: if the composite includes an extra element, it either enters the quiddity (making it subject to increase) or does not (making it irrelevant). He then focuses on the premise that if the external parts are the complete reality in the mind, then any other mental parts would be a second reality. An objector suggests that one reality could be external and the other mental, but the reply is that the external parts themselves constitute the mental reality, resulting in two mental realities. The commentator further refutes the claim that a quiddity can be composed of both types of parts by arguing that derivation (اشتقاق) removes a part from part-hood because it requires adding an external relation. Therefore, a composite of external parts cannot also be composed of logical parts, and a composite of logical parts is simple in the external. The argument concludes that the two types of composition are mutually exclusive.

Correction: The commentator argues that logical parts are the same external parts considered differently, not separate.

Key Takeaway

First Branch: Genus of Differentia Not Its Genus
First branch derived from causality of differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first branch derived from the causality of the differentia and why the genus of a differentia cannot serve as a genus for that same differentia.

Core Idea
The genus of a differentia cannot also be its genus—this prevents circularity in definitions.

In this lesson, we examine the first of four logical branches that follow from the principle that the differentia gives causality to the genus. The branch rules out a potential circularity where the genus of a differentia would itself need a genus, leading to an infinite regress. Understanding this rule helps maintain the hierarchical structure of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full English translation. Notice how the rule is stated and then clarified.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to how the commentator explains the wording.
2
Identify the Key Rule
Pinpoint the main statement: 'the genus of the differentia is not a genus for the differentia in consideration of two species.' Understand that this forbids combining two parts in one quiddity in this way.
3
Understand the Clarification
The commentator explains that when the text says 'a genus for the differentia,' it actually intends the differentia to be treated as a genus in relation to something else. This avoids the circularity by making the terms relative.
This clarifies why the rule does not lead to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفرعوا على علية الفصل كما فهموا فروعاً أربعة : الأول لا يكون فصل الجنس جنساً للفصل باعتبار (نوعين) أي لا يجوز أن يكون لماهية واحدة جزآن. قوله : (جنساً للفصل أراد بالفصل الجنس، وإنما عبر بالفصل؛ لأن المفروض أن يكون الفصل جنساً بالنسبة إليه، فيكون هو حينئذ فصلاً مقسماً بالنسبة إلى هذا الجنس.
And they derived four branches from the causality of the differentia, as they understood: the first is that the genus of the differentia is not a genus for the differentia in consideration of two species, i.e., it is not permissible for one quiddity to have two parts. His statement: 'a genus for the differentia' – he intended by 'the differentia' the genus, but he expressed it as the differentia because it is assumed that the differentia is a genus in relation to it, so then it would be a dividing differentia in relation to this genus.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four branches that scholars have derived from the principle that the differentia (فصل) has causality over the genus. The branch states that the genus of a differentia cannot itself be a genus for that differentia when considering two species. This means it is not possible for a single quiddity to have two parts (genus and differentia) in a way that would lead to an infinite regress or circularity, where the differentia's genus would also need a genus, and so on. The commentator clarifies the wording: the phrase 'a genus for the differentia' actually means that the differentia is being treated as a genus in relation to something else, and then that thing becomes a dividing differentia relative to this new genus. This avoids the problem by showing that the terms are relative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume the genus of a differentia can act as a genus for that differentia, leading to a circular definition.
Correction: The branch rules this out: the genus of a differentia cannot be a genus for that same differentia because it would create an infinite regress; instead, the terms are relative.

Checkpoint

A) It is the same as the differentia.
B) It cannot be a genus for that differentia.
C) It must be a species of that differentia.
D) It is a differentia for another genus.

Key Takeaway
The first branch maintains definitional hierarchy by preventing the genus of a differentia from also serving as its genus.

Refutation of Compatibility of Two Types of Composition
Refutes claim that composition from non-predicated parts can coexist with predicated parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why composition from non-predicated parts (like matter and form) cannot coexist with composition from predicated parts (genus and differentia).

Core Idea
Two types of composition are mutually exclusive; a thing cannot be composite in both ways simultaneously.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle objection: could an essence be composed of non-predicated parts (e.g., matter and form) and also be composed of predicated parts (genus and differentia)? He argues that this is impossible. By examining the process of derivation, he shows that any composite of predicated parts is ultimately simple in external reality. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Try to grasp the overall argument before diving into details.
Pay attention to the key terms: non-predicated parts, predicated parts, genus, differentia, derivation.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The author starts by stating that the claim that both types of composition can coexist is false. Find the sentence that introduces this claim.
The phrase 'فبطل ما قيل' indicates a refutation.
3
Understand the argument from derivation
The author explains that when a composite is derived from its shared part, it yields a genus; from its specific part, it yields a differentia. But derivation involves a relation external to the essence, which removes the part from being a part.
Consider why relating parts externally changes their status.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Conclude that a composite of non-predicated parts cannot be composed of predicated parts, and a composite of predicated parts is simple in external reality. This shows incompatibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مختلفتان في العقل، وأنه محال فبطل ما قيل من أن تركب الماهية من أجزاء غير محمولة لا ينافي تركبها من أجزاء محمولة، بل كل مركب خارجي إذا اشتق من جزئه المشترك بينه وبين غيره، كان ذلك المشتق جنساً له، وإذا اشتق من جزئه المختص به كان فصلاً له، وكل مركب فإنه مركب من الجنس والفصل، وكيف لا يبطل، والاشتقاق يخرج الجزء عن الجزئية؛ إذ لا بد أن يعتبر الجزء مع نسبة هي خارجة عن ماهية المركب، فإن النسبة بين الجزء والكل خارجة عنهما قطعاً، والجزء المأخوذ مع الخارج خارج. وتحقق عندك أن المركب من أجزاء غير محمولة لا يجوز أن يتركب من أجزاء محمولة وأن المركب من الأجزاء المحمولة لا يكون إلا بسيطاً في الخارج.
They are different in the intellect, and it is impossible. Therefore what was said that the composition of an essence from non-predicated parts does not preclude its composition from predicated parts is nullified. Rather, every external composite, when derived from its part shared between it and something else, that derived concept is a genus for it; and when derived from its specific part, it is a differentia for it. And every composite is composed of genus and differentia. How could it not be nullified? Derivation removes the part from part-hood; for it is necessary to consider the part with a relation that is external to the essence of the composite. Indeed, the relation between the part and the whole is external to both absolutely. And the part taken with something external is external. And it has been established for you that a composite of non-predicated parts cannot be composed of predicated parts, and that a composite of predicated parts is only simple in external reality.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a claim that composition from non-predicated parts (such as matter and form) is compatible with composition from predicated parts (genus and differentia). The author argues that the two are incompatible. He explains that when an external composite is considered via derivation from its shared part, that derivation yields a genus; from its specific part, it yields a differentia. Every composite is thus composed of genus and differentia. The derivation removes the part from being a part because it involves a relation external to the essence. Hence, a composite of non-predicated parts cannot also be composed of predicated parts; a composite of predicated parts is only simple in external reality.

Differentia Cannot Be Genus for Differentia
States first branch and resolves objection about animal and rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the differentia of a genus cannot serve as a genus for another differentia, and how the example of human, animal, and rational is resolved through distinguishing essential from accidental meanings.

Core Idea
A differentia cannot become a genus without causing circular causality.

This lesson examines the first consequence of the causality of the differentia: that a differentia cannot be a genus for another differentia. The argument is that if it could, then two parts of an essence would each cause the other, which is impossible. An objection using the classic example of human, animal, and rational is presented and resolved through careful distinction of meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that the passage begins with a derived branch: the differentia of a genus cannot be a genus for a differentia with respect to two species.
The text uses 'لا يكون' (cannot be) indicating a universal negative.
2
Understand the objection
The objection uses the example of human: animal is a genus shared with horse, rational is a differentia; but rational is a genus shared with angel, and animal becomes a differentia. See how this seems to violate the rule.
The reversal of roles between genus and differentia is the key.
3
Grasp the answer's distinction
The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'rational': as a substance (the rational soul) and as an accident (the power of intellection). Under the first meaning, it is not shared; under the second, it is not a differentia. Thus the objection fails.
This shows how careful definitions resolve logical puzzles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفرعوا على علية الفصل فروعاً أربعة: الأول لا يكون فصل الجنس جنساً للفصل باعتبار نوعين أي لا يجوز أن يكون لماهية واحدة جزآن، أحدهما جنس لها مشترك بينها وبين نوع ما، والآخر فصل لها يميزها عن النوع، ثم ينعكس الأمر فيكون هذا الفصل جنساً لها مشتركاً بينها وبين نوع آخر، وذلك الجنس فصلاً لها يميزها عن النوع الآخر، وإلا لكان كل منهما علة للآخر، وأنه محال. وأورد عليهم الحيوان والناطق؛ فإنه جنس للإنسان مشترك بينه وبين الفرس مثلا، والناطق فصل له يميزه عن الفرس، والناطق جنس له مشترك بينه وبين الملك، والحيوان فصل له يميزه عن الملك، فقد انعكس الحال بين الجنس والفصل في الإنسان بالقياس إلى نوعي الملك والفرس. وأجابوا عنه: بأن المراد بالناطق، إن كان هو الجوهر الذي له النطق أي إدراك المعقولات، فإنه ليس مشتركاً بين الإنسان والملك، بل مختلفاً بالماهية فيهما، فلا يكون جنساً لهما. وإن كان المراد بالناطق هو هذا العارض أعني مفهوم ما له قوة إدراك المعقولات، لم يكن فصلاً للإنسان، بل هو أثر من آثار فصله.
They derived from the causality of the differentia four branches: First, the differentia of a genus cannot be a genus for the differentia with respect to two species, meaning it is not permissible for one essence to have two parts, one a genus shared between it and some species, and the other a differentia distinguishing it from the species, then the situation reverses so that this differentia becomes a genus shared between it and another species, and that genus becomes a differentia distinguishing it from the other species, otherwise each would be a cause of the other, and that is impossible. An objection was raised against them: animal and rational; for animal is a genus for human shared between him and, say, horse, and rational is a differentia distinguishing him from horse; rational is a genus for human shared between him and angel, and animal is a differentia distinguishing him from angel; so the situation has reversed between genus and differentia in human with respect to the two species of angel and horse. They answered: if by 'rational' is meant the substance that has rationality, i.e., intellection of intelligibles, then it is not shared between human and angel, but differs in essence in both, so it is not a genus for them. And if by 'rational' is meant this accident, namely the concept of that which has the power of intellection, then it is not a differentia for human, but rather an effect of his differentia.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch of the four consequences derived from the causality of the differentia. It states that it is impossible for one essence to have two parts such that one is a genus for that essence shared with another species, and the other is a differentia for that essence distinguishing it from that species, and then the roles reverse with respect to another species. This would lead to each being the cause of the other, which is impossible. An objection is raised using the example of human: 'animal' seems to be a genus shared with horse, and 'rational' a differentia distinguishing from horse; but when compared to angel, 'rational' seems to be a genus shared with angel, and 'animal' a differentia distinguishing from angel. The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'rational'. If it means the substantial form that is the principle of intellection (the rational soul), then it is not shared between human and angel because the essences differ. If it means the accidental property of having the power of intellection, then it is not a differentia but an effect of the true differentia. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms

جنس

نوع

ناطق
جوهر
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example of animal and rational shows that a differentia can be a genus for another differentia, refuting the rule.
Correction: The apparent counterexample fails because 'rational' is ambiguous: as a substance it is not shared, as an accident it is not the real differentia. The rule remains valid.

Checkpoint

D) The species would multiply.

Key Takeaway
The differentia cannot be a genus for a differentia because it would lead to circular causality, and apparent counterexamples are resolved by distinguishing essential from accidental meanings.

Proximate Differentia Cannot Be Multiple
States the rule that a single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae and how this relates to causal independence.

Core Idea
One thing, one proximate differentia — two would cause impossible causal conflict.

Islamic logic carefully defines how we classify things. One key rule is that a single species cannot have two proximate differentiae at the same level. This lesson explains why that is impossible and what would go wrong if we allowed it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the rule itself.
Notice the phrase 'in one rank' — this allows different differentiae at different levels.
2
Understand the rule
The passage states a prohibition: one thing cannot have two proximate differentiae. Identify the logical reason given.
3
Examine the consequence
The passage warns that if there were two, two independent causes would converge on one effect. Reflect on why that is problematic in logic.
This connects to the principle of causality.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني: الفصل القريب لا يتعدد، فلا يكون لشيء واحد فصلان قريبان أي في مرتبة واحدة وإلا اجتمع على المعلول الواحد بالذات علتان مستقلتان.
The second branch: The proximate differentia does not multiply, so there cannot be for one thing two proximate differentiae, i.e., in one rank, otherwise there would converge on one effect per se two independent causes.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage states the second branch: a single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae at the same level of generality (i.e., in one rank). If it did, then two independent causes would converge on one effect, which is impossible. The phrase 'in one rank' allows for different differentiae at different ranks (e.g., sensitive and rational) because that does not produce two independent causes in the same way.

Key Terms
الفصل القريب
علتان مستقلتان
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be multiple.
B) It cannot be multiple.
C) It can be multiple if in different ranks.
D) It is always the same as the genus.

Key Takeaway
A single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae in the same rank, as that would imply two independent causes for one effect.

Resolving the Inversion Objection: Ambiguity of 'Rational'
Objection and two-part answer on inversion of genus and differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection to the non-inversion of genus and differentia is resolved by distinguishing two meanings of the term 'rational'.

Core Idea
Apparent logical contradictions dissolve when ambiguous terms are properly distinguished.

In this passage, the author presents a clever objection that seems to show genus and differentia can reverse roles. The example of 'animal' and 'rational' in relation to man, horse, and angel is used to argue that the same term can serve as both genus and differentia. The answer resolves the challenge by carefully analyzing what 'rational' truly means—either a substance or an accident—and showing that neither interpretation supports the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection closely
Read the first part of the passage where the objector uses 'animal' and 'rational' to claim an inversion. Notice how each term plays opposite roles when comparing man to horse versus man to angel.
The objector's aim is to show that the division between genus and differentia is not fixed.
2
Examine the first answer (substantial interpretation)
Read the answer beginning with 'إن كان المراد بالناطق هو الجوهر...'. Understand that when 'rational' means the substance with the power of intellection, it is not common between man and angel; thus it fails as a genus.
This interpretation blocks the inversion by denying the shared genus.
3
Examine the second answer (accidental interpretation)
Read the answer beginning with 'وإن كان المراد بالناطق هو هذا العارض...'. Understand that when 'rational' means the accident of having the power of intellection, it is not a true differentia but merely an effect; thus it fails as a differentia.
This interpretation blocks the inversion by denying the differentia role.
4
Synthesize the resolution
Combine the two answers to see why the objection fails. The objector's argument relies on an ambiguity: it uses 'rational' inconsistently. Once we fix a single meaning, the inversion disappears.
This shows the importance of precision in definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد عليهم الحيوان والناطق؛ فإنه جنس للإنسان مشترك به وبين الفرس مثلا، والناطق فصل له يميزه عن الفرس، والناطق جنس له مشترك بينه وبين الملك، والحيوان فصل له يميزه عن الملك، فقد انعكس الحال بين الجنس والفصل في الإنسان بالقياس إلى نوعي الملك والفرس. وأجابوا عنه: بأن المراد بالناطق، إن كان هو الجوهر الذي له النطق أي إدراك المعقولات، فإنه ليس مشتركاً بين الإنسان والملك، بل مختلفاً بالماهية فيهما، فلا يكون جنساً لهما. وإن كان المراد بالناطق هو هذا العارض أعني مفهوم ما له قوة إدراك المعقولات، لم يكن فصلاً للإنسان، بل هو أثر من آثار فصله.
They objected to them [by citing] 'animal' and 'rational': for it is a genus for man, common between him and the horse for example, and 'rational' is a differentia distinguishing him from the horse; and 'rational' is a genus for him, common between him and the angel, and 'animal' is a differentia distinguishing him from the angel; thus the situation between genus and differentia in man with respect to the two species, angel and horse, is reversed. And they answered: that by 'rational', if what is meant is the substance that possesses rationality, i.e., the apprehension of intelligibles, then it is not common between man and angel, but rather different in quiddity in both, so it is not a genus for them. And if what is meant by 'rational' is this accident, namely the concept of that which has the power to apprehend intelligibles, then it is not a differentia for man, but rather an effect of his differentia.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the relationship between genus and differentia. The objection attempts to show that the roles of genus and differentia can invert by considering the example of 'animal' and 'rational' in relation to man, horse, and angel. The objector argues that 'animal' is a genus for man shared with the horse, and 'rational' is a differentia distinguishing man from the horse; but then 'rational' becomes a genus for man shared with the angel, and 'animal' becomes a differentia distinguishing man from the angel. Thus the same term (rational) is both a genus (relative to angel) and a differentia (relative to horse). The answer provides a resolution by distinguishing two meanings of 'rational'. First, if 'rational' means the substance (the soul or substantial form) that has the power of intellection, then it is different in quiddity between man and angel, so it is not truly a genus common to both. Second, if 'rational' means the accident (the attribute of having the power of intellection), then it is not a differentia for man but rather a necessary consequence (effect) of the true differentia. Therefore, the objection does not hold.

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
ناطق
جوهر
عارض
أثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That genus and differentia can genuinely invert roles in the same subject.
Correction: The apparent inversion arises from ambiguity in the term 'rational', which can mean either a substance or an accident. When each meaning is pinned down, the inversion disappears.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the objection, what is the second interpretation of 'rational'?
A) The substance that possesses rationality
B) The accident of having the power of intellection
C) The genus of man
D) The differentia of the horse

Key Takeaway
Ambiguity in the term 'rational' explains the apparent inversion of genus and differentia; precise definition resolves the conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a contradiction in your understanding of a religious text or doctrine, do you first pause to examine whether the terms involved might have multiple meanings? How often do you assume that a word means the same thing every time, and how has that assumption led you astray?
?
Is your intellectual humility deep enough to admit that your apparent objections might dissolve once you define your terms more carefully? Write down one area in your faith where you feel a tension—then ask yourself: which term might I be misunderstanding?
?
You claim to love the truth, but do you love it enough to dissect your own arguments and expose where they rely on ambiguous language? What will it cost you to stop using words carelessly, and are you willing to pay that price starting today?

The Proximate Differentia Cannot Be Multiple: The Foundational Principle
Sanusi states the principle and gives the proof based on independent causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational principle that a single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae in one rank and the rational proof behind it.

Core Idea
Two proximate differentiae would mean two independent causes for one effect, which is impossible.

Sanusi opens this branch by stating a strict logical principle: no single thing can have two proximate differentiae in the same rank. The danger, he warns, is that this would entail two independent causes acting on one essence — an impossibility in causal reasoning. This lesson presents the principle and its supporting argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the structure: principle, proof, then glosses.
The glosses clarify the scope of the principle.
2
Identify the principle and its proof
Locate the statement that two proximate differentiae are impossible and the reason given: otherwise two independent causes would converge on one effect.
3
Understand the restriction to 'one rank'
The passage explains that multiple differentiae are allowed if they are in different ranks (e.g., rational for man, sensitive for animal). This nuance is essential.
The restriction prevents the principle from being too broad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني: الفصل القريب لا يتعدد، فلا يكون لشيء واحد فصلان قريبان في مرتبة واحدة، وإلا اجتمع على المعلول الواحد بالذات علتان مستقلتان. قوله: (والحيوان فصل له إلخ) لعدم وجود النمو في الملك، وإن كان حساساً متحركاً بالإرادة على رأي المتكلمين. قوله: (إن كان هو الجوهر إلخ) اللام للعهد أي ذلك الجوهر الذي هو مبدأ النطق في الإنسان، وهو صورته النوعية أو النفس الناطقة، وحينئذ لا شك في أنه ليس مشتركاً، وبعضهم حمله على الجنس وأول العبارة الدالة على ادعاء الاشتراك بالمنع أي لا نسلم اشتراكه لم يجوز أن يكون مختلفاً فيهما. وهذا القدر كاف في دفع النقض. قوله: (بل هو أثر من آثار فصله) ويجوز اشتراك المتخالفين في عارض واحد كما مر. قوله: (في مرتبة واحدة) قيد بذلك؛ لأنه يجوز تعدده لماهية واحدة إذا كانا مرتبتين بأن يكون أحدهما فصلاً قريباً لجنس، والآخر لجنس آخر فوقه نحو الناطق والحساس. قوله: (وإلا لكان كل منهما علة للآخر) قيل: لم لا يجوز أن تكون ذات كل منهما علة لحصة الآخر بلا استحالة وأجيب بأن التفريع المذكور بناء على ما فهموا من علية الفصل لطبيعة الجنس، فإن الدليل المذكور على تقدير تمامه إنما يدل على هذا. قوله: (فإنه ليس مشتركاً بل مختلفاً) هذا على سبيل المنع أي لا نسلم الاشتراك، فإن الأصل لما كان ثابتاً بالدليل على زعم المستدل، وكان الإيراد نقضاً عليه، كفى في الجواب منع الاشتراك بلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال باختلاف الآثار. قوله: (بل هو أثر من آثار فصله) إذا سلم اشتراك هذا العارض كما هو الظاهر، لم يكن أثراً لفصله القريب، فلا بد أن يقيد بشيء لا يوجد في الملك، فتأمل. ثم قال: الفصل بالقريب؛ لأن الفصل البعيد وكذا المطلق يجوز تعدده، ويكون كل من الفصول المتعددة علة للجنس الذي في مرتبته كالناطق للحيوان والحساس للجسم النامي، والنامي للجسم مطلقاً، وقابل الأبعاد للجوهر. واعتبر وحدة المعلول بالذات؛ لأنه إذا تعدد ذاته جاز توارد العلل عليه كما في أفراد نوع واحد يقع بعضها بعلة وبعضها بعلة أخرى، وأما مع وحدة الذات، فلا مساغ لذلك؛ إذ يستغنى بكل عن كل، سواء كان الواحد بالذات شخصاً، وهو ظاهر، أو لا، كما نحن بصدده، فإن طبيعة الجنس في النوع قبل اعتبار تعدد أفراده ذات واحدة لا تعدد فيها، وقيد العلة بالاستقلال؛ لأن تعدد العلل الناقصة جائز، فإن قلت: ليس الفصل وحده علة تامة للجنس؛ لجواز أن يكون للجنس أجزاء، وأن يكون هناك شرائط معتبرة. قلت: كل واحد من الفصلين مع باقي الأمور المعتبرة علة مستقلة فيلزم توارد العلل المستقلة. لا يقال: الحساس والمتحرك بالإرادة فصلان قريبان للحيوان؛ لأنا نقول: بل كل منهما أثر لفصله، فإن حقيقة الفصل إذا جهلت عبر عنها بأقرب آثارها كالنطق لفصل الإنسان. ولما اشتبه تقدم كل من الحس والحركة الإرادية على الآخر عبر بهما. ولم يتعرض الشارح قدس سره لبيان فائدته؛ لأن بيان فائدة قيد القريب يتضمنه فإن الفصل البعيد قريب في مرتبة الجنس البعيد. قوله: (وأما مع وحدة الذات إلخ) يعني أن الدليل الذي ذكروه في امتناع توارد العلل، وإن صوره في الواحد الشخصي، لكنه جار في الواحد بالذات، سواء كان شخصاً أو لا. قوله: (فإن طبيعة الجنس في النوع) أي الواحد ذات واحدة بخلافها في النوعين، فإنها متحصصة، ففي كل نوع يكون الفصل علة لحصتها، فلا يكون المعلول واحداً بالذات، وتوارد الفصول مع تخصص الجنس ليس أحدها متقدماً على الآخر، فتدبر. قوله: (كل واحد من الفصلين إلخ) حاصله: أنه كما يمتنع توارد التامتين يمتنع توارد الناقصتين من جنس واحد كالفاعلين والمادتين والصورتين؛ لاستلزامه توارد التامتين، وفيما نحن فيه على قاعدة العلية يكون الفصل علة فاعلية؛ إذ العلة الموجبة إذا كان أمراً واحداً لا يكون إلا فاعلاً. قوله: (أثر لفصله) فالفصل واحد عبر عنه باللازمين لكونهما في مرتبة واحدة. قوله: (ولما اشتبه تقدم إلخ) إذ الإحساس قد يكون مبدأ للحركة، وقد تكون الحركة مبدأ للإحساس. قوله: (ولما اشتبه تقدم كل من الحس والحركة إلخ) قيل: يتقدم الإحساس على الحركة الإرادية لأنه إدراك وهي متوقفة عليه، ورد بأن الموقوف عليه هو الإدراك مطلقاً، لا الإحساس. وأيضاً الإنسان ربما يتحرك إلى شيء ليدركه فبعض الحركة متقدم على الإدراك، فلم يظهر تقدم أحدهما على الآخر على الإطلاق، فوضع الكل موضع الفصل. واعلم أنه لا بد من تقييد الحركة
The second branch: The proximate differentia is not multiple, so a single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae in one rank, otherwise two independent causes would converge on a single effect in essence. His statement: 'And animal is a differentia for him, etc.' because of the absence of growth in the angel, even though it is sentient and volitionally moving according to the theologians. His statement: 'If it is the substance, etc.' the lam is for the covenant meaning that substance which is the principle of rationality in man, which is his specific form or the rational soul, and then there is no doubt that it is not common, and some interpreted it as genus and interpreted the statement indicating the claim of commonality as denial, i.e., we do not accept its commonality, nor do we allow it to be different in both. And this is sufficient to repel the objection. His statement: 'Rather it is an effect of his differentia' and it is permissible for differing things to share in a single accident as previously. His statement: 'In one rank' restricts it with that because it is permissible for it to be multiple for a single quiddity if they are in two ranks such that one is a proximate differentia for a genus, and the other for another genus above it like 'rational' and 'sensitive'. His statement: 'Otherwise each of them would be a cause of the other' it is said: why is it not permissible for the essence of each to be a cause of the portion of the other without impossibility? And it is answered that the aforementioned deduction is based on what they understood from the causality of the differentia for the nature of the genus, for the aforementioned proof, if complete, only indicates this. His statement: 'It is not common but different' this is by way of denial, i.e., we do not accept commonality, for since the original is established by proof according to the claimant, and the objection is a counterexample against it, it suffices in the answer to deny commonality without need to argue by difference of effects. His statement: 'Rather it is an effect of his differentia' if the commonality of this accident is accepted as apparent, it would not be an effect of his proximate differentia, so it must be restricted by something not found in the angel, so contemplate. Then he said: The restriction to the proximate [differentia] is because the remote differentia as well as the absolute [differentia] can be multiple, and each of the multiple differentiae is a cause for the genus in its rank, like 'rational' for animal, 'sensitive' for the growing body, 'growing' for body absolutely, and 'receptive of dimensions' for substance. And he considered the unity of the effect in essence because if its essence is multiple, the convergence of causes upon it is permissible as in individuals of a single species, some occurring by one cause and some by another. But with unity of essence, there is no way for that because each would be sufficient without the other, whether the one in essence is a person, which is apparent, or not, as in our case, for the nature of the genus in the species before considering the multiplicity of its individuals is a single essence with no multiplicity. And he restricted the cause by independence because the multiplicity of incomplete causes is permissible. If you say: the differentia alone is not a complete cause for the genus because it is possible for the genus to have parts and that there are conditions considered. I say: each of the two differentiae together with the remaining considered conditions is an independent cause, so the convergence of independent causes is entailed. It cannot be said: 'sensitive' and 'volitionally moving' are two proximate differentiae for animal because we say: rather each of them is an effect of its differentia, for if the reality of the differentia is unknown, it is expressed by its nearest effects, like 'rational' for the differentia of man. And since the precedence of each of sensation and volitional movement over the other was ambiguous, they expressed by both. And the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) did not address the benefit of his restriction because the explanation of the benefit of the restriction to the proximate is implied by it, for the remote differentia is proximate in the rank of the remote genus. His statement: 'And as for with unity of essence, etc.' means that the proof they mentioned for the impossibility of convergence of causes, although they formulated it in the personal one, it is valid for the one in essence, whether it is a person or not. His statement: 'The nature of the genus in the species' i.e., the one is a single essence unlike it in two species, for it is particularized, so in each species the differentia is a cause for its portion, so the effect is not one in essence, and the convergence of differentiae with the specification of the genus is not one preceding the other, so reflect. His statement: 'Each of the two differentiae, etc.' its summary: just as the convergence of two complete [causes] is impossible, so is the convergence of two incomplete [causes] of the same kind, like two agents, two materials, and two forms, because it necessitates the convergence of two complete [causes]. And in our case, on the basis of causality, the differentia is an efficient cause, for the necessitating cause, if it is one thing, can only be an agent. His statement: 'Effect of his differentia' so the differentia is one, expressed by the two necessary concomitants because they are in one rank. His statement: 'And since the precedence was ambiguous, etc.' for sensation may be a principle of movement, and movement may be a principle of sensation. His statement: 'And since the precedence of each of sensation and movement was ambiguous, etc.' it is said: sensation precedes volitional movement because it is perception and movement depends on it, and it is refuted by saying that what is depended upon is perception absolutely, not sensation. Also, man may move towards something to perceive it, so some movement precedes perception, so it is not apparent that one of them precedes the other absolutely, so they placed the whole in place of the differentia. And know that it is necessary to restrict movement...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument for the principle that a single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae. It begins by stating the principle and the proof: if there were two, then two independent causes would converge on one effect. Then it addresses several glosses: first, explaining why animal is a differentia for man relative to angel (due to lack of growth in angels). Then it explains the meaning of 'rational' as substance versus accident. Then it explains the restriction to 'one rank' because differentiae can be multiple in different ranks. Then it addresses an objection that if two differentiae were possible, each would be cause of the other, and answers that the causation relation prevents this. Then it discusses the condition of unity of essence and independence of causes, showing that the proof applies to both specific and personal unity. It also addresses the example of 'sensitive' and 'volitionally moving' as possible proximate differentiae for animal, but explains they are merely effects of a single differentia. Finally, it discusses the ambiguity of precedence between sensation and movement.

Key Terms
فصل قريب
علة مستقلة
معلول واحد بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a single thing not have two proximate differentiae in one rank?
A) Because the differentia must be one in number
B) Because two independent causes would converge on one effect
C) Because the differentia is always a species
D) Because the differentia must be an accident

Key Takeaway
A single essence cannot have two proximate differentiae in one rank because that would require two independent causes for one effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that a single essence cannot have two proximate differentiae in one rank. In your own life, do you ever try to define yourself by two independent, contradictory qualities at the same level? How might that lead to internal confusion, and what would it mean to resolve that by ranking them properly?
?
The principle here is based on the impossibility of two independent causes for one effect. Do you recognize any area of your life where you treat your happiness or success as having two independent ultimate causes, such as both worldly achievement and divine pleasure? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what would it look like to truly make one the cause and the other a secondary effect?
?
Sanusi argues that 'rational' is not common between humans and angels. Are you tempted to think that your rationality makes you like the angels, forgetting that your distinct differentia (as a human) includes growth and other material attributes? How can embracing your specific differentia—with all its limitations—lead you to greater humility and reliance on Allah?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate

Conditions for the Impossibility: Unity of Essence and Independence of Causes
Sanusi explains the conditions of unity of essence and independence of causes for the principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key conditions that must hold for the principle to apply: unity of essence of the effect and independence of the causes.

Core Idea
The proof only holds when the effect is one in essence and the causes are each independent.

Having established the principle, Sanusi now refines it by specifying two crucial conditions: the effect must be one in essence (واحد بالذات), and the causes must be independent (مستقلة). Without these, multiple differentiae could coexist without contradiction. This lesson unpacks these conditions and why they matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on conditions
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two conditions: unity of essence and independence of causes.
2
Distinguish between unity of essence and personal unity
The passage explains that the effect must be one in essence, not just one individual. This is a more general condition.
Personal unity is a special case of unity of essence.
3
Understand why incomplete causes are allowed
The author clarifies that the condition is about independent causes; incomplete causes (with conditions) can still be multiple if they are not independent.
The differentia alone is not a complete cause; it requires other conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المتخالفين في عارض واحد كما مر. قوله: (في مرتبة واحدة) قيد بذلك؛ لأنه يجوز تعدده لماهية واحدة إذا كانا مرتبتين بأن يكون أحدهما فصلاً قريباً لجنس، والآخر لجنس آخر فوقه نحو الناطق والحساس. قوله: (وإلا لكان كل منهما علة للآخر) قيل: لم لا يجوز أن تكون ذات كل منهما علة لحصة الآخر بلا استحالة وأجيب بأن التفريع المذكور بناء على ما فهموا من علية الفصل لطبيعة الجنس، فإن الدليل المذكور على تقدير تمامه إنما يدل على هذا. قوله: (فإنه ليس مشتركاً بل مختلفاً) هذا على سبيل المنع أي لا نسلم الاشتراك، فإن الأصل لما كان ثابتاً بالدليل على زعم المستدل، وكان الإيراد نقضاً عليه، كفى في الجواب منع الاشتراك بلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال باختلاف الآثار. قوله: (بل هو أثر من آثار فصله) إذا سلم اشتراك هذا العارض كما هو الظاهر، لم يكن أثراً لفصله القريب، فلا بد أن يقيد بشيء لا يوجد في الملك، فتأمل. ثم قال: الفصل بالقريب؛ لأن الفصل البعيد وكذا المطلق يجوز تعدده، ويكون كل من الفصول المتعددة علة للجنس الذي في مرتبته كالناطق للحيوان والحساس للجسم النامي، والنامي للجسم مطلقاً، وقابل الأبعاد للجوهر. واعتبر وحدة المعلول بالذات؛ لأنه إذا تعدد ذاته جاز توارد العلل عليه كما في أفراد نوع واحد يقع بعضها بعلة وبعضها بعلة أخرى، وأما مع وحدة الذات، فلا مساغ لذلك؛ إذ يستغنى بكل عن كل، سواء كان الواحد بالذات شخصاً، وهو ظاهر، أو لا، كما نحن بصدده، فإن طبيعة الجنس في النوع قبل اعتبار تعدد أفراده ذات واحدة لا تعدد فيها، وقيد العلة بالاستقلال؛ لأن تعدد العلل الناقصة جائز، فإن قلت: ليس الفصل وحده علة تامة للجنس؛ لجواز أن يكون للجنس أجزاء، وأن يكون هناك شرائط معتبرة. قلت: كل واحد من الفصلين مع باقي الأمور المعتبرة علة مستقلة فيلزم توارد العلل المستقلة. لا يقال: الحساس والمتحرك بالإرادة فصلان قريبان للحيوان؛ لأنا نقول: بل كل منهما أثر لفصله، فإن حقيقة الفصل إذا جهلت عبر عنها بأقرب آثارها كالنطق لفصل الإنسان. ولما اشتبه تقدم كل من الحس والحركة الإرادية على الآخر عبر بهما. ولم يتعرض الشارح قدس سره لبيان فائدته؛ لأن بيان فائدة قيد القريب يتضمنه فإن الفصل البعيد قريب في مرتبة الجنس البعيد. قوله: (وأما مع وحدة الذات إلخ) يعني أن الدليل الذي ذكروه في امتناع توارد العلل، وإن صوره في الواحد الشخصي، لكنه جار في الواحد بالذات، سواء كان شخصاً أو لا. قوله: (فإن طبيعة الجنس في النوع) أي الواحد ذات واحدة بخلافها في النوعين، فإنها متحصصة، ففي كل نوع يكون الفصل علة لحصتها، فلا يكون المعلول واحداً بالذات، وتوارد الفصول مع تخصص الجنس ليس أحدها متقدماً على الآخر، فتدبر. قوله: (كل واحد من الفصلين إلخ) حاصله: أنه كما يمتنع توارد التامتين يمتنع توارد الناقصتين من جنس واحد كالفاعلين والمادتين والصورتين؛ لاستلزامه توارد التامتين، وفيما نحن فيه على قاعدة العلية يكون الفصل علة فاعلية؛ إذ العلة الموجبة إذا كان أمراً واحداً لا يكون إلا فاعلاً. قوله: (أثر لفصله) فالفصل واحد عبر عنه باللازمين لكونهما في مرتبة واحدة. قوله: (ولما اشتبه تقدم إلخ) إذ الإحساس قد يكون مبدأ للحركة، وقد تكون الحركة مبدأ للإحساس. قوله: (ولما اشتبه تقدم كل من الحس والحركة إلخ) قيل: يتقدم الإحساس على الحركة الإرادية لأنه إدراك وهي متوقفة عليه، ورد بأن الموقوف عليه هو الإدراك مطلقاً، لا الإحساس. وأيضاً الإنسان ربما يتحرك إلى شيء ليدركه فبعض الحركة متقدم على الإدراك، فلم يظهر تقدم أحدهما على الآخر على الإطلاق، فوضع الكل موضع الفصل. واعلم أنه لا بد من تقييد الحركة
The second branch: The proximate differentia is not multiple, so a single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae in one rank, otherwise two independent causes would converge on a single effect in essence. His statement: 'And animal is a differentia for him, etc.' because of the absence of growth in the angel, even though it is sentient and volitionally moving according to the theologians. His statement: 'If it is the substance, etc.' the lam is for the covenant meaning that substance which is the principle of rationality in man, which is his specific form or the rational soul, and then there is no doubt that it is not common, and some interpreted it as genus and interpreted the statement indicating the claim of commonality as denial, i.e., we do not accept its commonality, nor do we allow it to be different in both. And this is sufficient to repel the objection. His statement: 'Rather it is an effect of his differentia' and it is permissible for differing things to share in a single accident as previously. His statement: 'In one rank' restricts it with that because it is permissible for it to be multiple for a single quiddity if they are in two ranks such that one is a proximate differentia for a genus, and the other for another genus above it like 'rational' and 'sensitive'. His statement: 'Otherwise each of them would be a cause of the other' it is said: why is it not permissible for the essence of each to be a cause of the portion of the other without impossibility? And it is answered that the aforementioned deduction is based on what they understood from the causality of the differentia for the nature of the genus, for the aforementioned proof, if complete, only indicates this. His statement: 'It is not common but different' this is by way of denial, i.e., we do not accept commonality, for since the original is established by proof according to the claimant, and the objection is a counterexample against it, it suffices in the answer to deny commonality without need to argue by difference of effects. His statement: 'Rather it is an effect of his differentia' if the commonality of this accident is accepted as apparent, it would not be an effect of his proximate differentia, so it must be restricted by something not found in the angel, so contemplate. Then he said: The restriction to the proximate [differentia] is because the remote differentia as well as the absolute [differentia] can be multiple, and each of the multiple differentiae is a cause for the genus in its rank, like 'rational' for animal, 'sensitive' for the growing body, 'growing' for body absolutely, and 'receptive of dimensions' for substance. And he considered the unity of the effect in essence because if its essence is multiple, the convergence of causes upon it is permissible as in individuals of a single species, some occurring by one cause and some by another. But with unity of essence, there is no way for that because each would be sufficient without the other, whether the one in essence is a person, which is apparent, or not, as in our case, for the nature of the genus in the species before considering the multiplicity of its individuals is a single essence with no multiplicity. And he restricted the cause by independence because the multiplicity of incomplete causes is permissible. If you say: the differentia alone is not a complete cause for the genus because it is possible for the genus to have parts and that there are conditions considered. I say: each of the two differentiae together with the remaining considered conditions is an independent cause, so the convergence of independent causes is entailed. It cannot be said: 'sensitive' and 'volitionally moving' are two proximate differentiae for animal because we say: rather each of them is an effect of its differentia, for if the reality of the differentia is unknown, it is expressed by its nearest effects, like 'rational' for the differentia of man. And since the precedence of each of sensation and volitional movement over the other was ambiguous, they expressed by both. And the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) did not address the benefit of his restriction because the explanation of the benefit of the restriction to the proximate is implied by it, for the remote differentia is proximate in the rank of the remote genus. His statement: 'And as for with unity of essence, etc.' means that the proof they mentioned for the impossibility of convergence of causes, although they formulated it in the personal one, it is valid for the one in essence, whether it is a person or not. His statement: 'The nature of the genus in the species' i.e., the one is a single essence unlike it in two species, for it is particularized, so in each species the differentia is a cause for its portion, so the effect is not one in essence, and the convergence of differentiae with the specification of the genus is not one preceding the other, so reflect. His statement: 'Each of the two differentiae, etc.' its summary: just as the convergence of two complete [causes] is impossible, so is the convergence of two incomplete [causes] of the same kind, like two agents, two materials, and two forms, because it necessitates the convergence of two complete [causes]. And in our case, on the basis of causality, the differentia is an efficient cause, for the necessitating cause, if it is one thing, can only be an agent. His statement: 'Effect of his differentia' so the differentia is one, expressed by the two necessary concomitants because they are in one rank. His statement: 'And since the precedence was ambiguous, etc.' for sensation may be a principle of movement, and movement may be a principle of sensation. His statement: 'And since the precedence of each of sensation and movement was ambiguous, etc.' it is said: sensation precedes volitional movement because it is perception and movement depends on it, and it is refuted by saying that what is depended upon is perception absolutely, not sensation. Also, man may move towards something to perceive it, so some movement precedes perception, so it is not apparent that one of them precedes the other absolutely, so they placed the whole in place of the differentia. And know that it is necessary to restrict movement...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument for the principle that a single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae. It begins by stating the principle and the proof: if there were two, then two independent causes would converge on one effect. Then it addresses several glosses: first, explaining why animal is a differentia for man relative to angel (due to lack of growth in angels). Then it explains the meaning of 'rational' as substance versus accident. Then it explains the restriction to 'one rank' because differentiae can be multiple in different ranks. Then it addresses an objection that if two differentiae were possible, each would be cause of the other, and answers that the causation relation prevents this. Then it discusses the condition of unity of essence and independence of causes, showing that the proof applies to both specific and personal unity. It also addresses the example of 'sensitive' and 'volitionally moving' as possible proximate differentiae for animal, but explains they are merely effects of a single differentia. Finally, it discusses the ambiguity of precedence between sensation and movement.

Key Terms
